Taharah — Purification
intermediate

Definition and Conditions of Iqrar (Confession)
Defines iqrar legally and linguistically, states its conditions and evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of iqrar, identify its conditions, and explain the evidence from Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
Iqrar is a report (ikhbar) of a right of another on oneself, not a creation; valid even from a slave but deferred when suspicion.

This lesson opens the Book of Iqrar, a key topic in Hanafi jurisprudence. The author defines iqrar linguistically and technically, distinguishing it from testimony and creation. You will learn the conditions for validity, the pillar, the ruling, and the evidence from Quran, Sunnah, ijma, and reason.

Guided Path
1
Dissect the linguistic and technical definition
Identify the components of the definition: ikhbar (report), thubut haqq (establishment of a right), 'ala nafsihi (against oneself). Note that it is a report (ikhbar), not a creation (insha').
The definition excludes testimony (shahada) and claim (da'wa).
2
Identify the conditions of validity
List the conditions: adult (baligh), sane ('aqil), free-willed (mukhtar). Note that freedom is NOT a condition, but the slave's iqrar has special rules.
The condition 'free' in the matn is to indicate that the slave's iqrar is deferred in some cases.
3
Understand the pillar and ruling
The pillar (rukn) is a word (lafz) or its equivalent (like writing to an absent person). The ruling (hukm) is the appearance (zuhur) of the confessed right, not its initial creation.
If the iqrar is valid, the right becomes established against the confessor.
4
Trace the evidence
Identify the four proofs: Quran 2:282 (command to dictate implies acceptance of iqrar), Sunnah (stories of Ma'iz and Ghamidiyyah), Ijma (consensus of the ummah), and rational argument (a sane person does not falsely confess to harm).
The 'wajh al-dalalah' from the Quran is that the command would be meaningless if iqrar were not accepted.
5
Distinguish the slave's iqrar
Apply the concept of tuhma (suspicion). For matters without tuhma (hadd, qisas), the slave's iqrar is immediately effective. For financial matters with tuhma, it is deferred until after emancipation.
The slave retains original freedom in hadd/qisas, hence no tuhma.

Source Text

هو إخبار عن ثبوت حق الغير على نفسه إذا أقر حر مكلف
أي الإقرار في الشرع

كتاب الإقرار هو في اللغة الإثبات يقال: قر الشيء إذا ثبت وأقر غيره إذا أثبته عيني)] مشتق من القرار، فكان في اللغة: عبارة عن إثبات ما كان متزلزلاً بين الجحود والثبوت وفي الشرع: إخبار أي ليس بإنشاء، وإعلام بالقول، فلو كتب أو أشار و لم يقل شيئاً لم يكن إقراراً، إلا إذا كان المقرله غائباً فكتب إليه: أما بعد فلك علي كذا فإنه يكون كالقول، وشرط صحته أن يكون المقر بالغاً عاقلاً طائعاً، وكونه حراً ليس بشرط، حتى يصح إقرار العبد، فينفذ في الحال فيما لا تهمة فيه كالحدود والقصاص، وفيما فيه تهمة لا يؤاخذ به في الحال؛ لأنه إقرار على المولى، ويؤاخذ به بعد العتق؛ لزوال المانع.
وركنه لفظ أو ما في حكمه دال عليه وحكمه ظهور المقر به لا ثبوته ابتداء، ودليله الكتاب، وهو قوله تعالى: وَلْيُمْلِلِ الَّذِي عَلَيْهِ الْحَقُّ ﴾ (البقرة: ۲۸۲) أمره بالإملال، فلو لم يقبل إقراره لما كان لإملاله معنى، والسنة: لرجم ماعز والغامدية بإقرارهما، والإجماع؛ لأن الأمة أجمعت على أن الإقرار حجة في حق نفسه، والمعقول؛ لأن العاقل لا يقر على نفسه كاذباً فيما فيه ضرر على نفسه أو ماله فترجحت جهة الصدق في حق نفسه؛ لعدم التهمة، وكمال الولاية. (فتح) هو إخبار إلخ وهذا كالجنس؛ لأنه يتناول الشهادة، فإنها أيضاً إخبار عن ثبوت حق الغير. ذلك، وهذا شأن الحد أن يكون مشتملاً على الجنس والفصل، وقيده بأن يكون على نفسه؛
على نفسه يخرج لأنه لو كان على غيره لغيره يكون شهادة، ولنفسه يكون دعوى، وأطلق الحق في قوله : "ثبوت حق"؛ ليشتمل ما لو كان الحق المقر به من قبيل الإسقاطات كالطلاق والعتاق؛ إذ الطلاق رفع القيد الثابت شرعاً بالنكاح، فإذا أقر بالطلاق يثبت للمرأة من الحق ما لم يكن لها من قبل. (فتح، عيني)
إذا أقر حر: قيد به؛ ليصح إقراره مطلقاً، واحترز به عن العبد؛ لأن العبد المحجور عليه يتأخر إقراره بالمال إلى ما بعد العتق، وكذا المأذون له يتأخر إقراره بما ليس من باب التجارة كإقراره بالمهر بوطء امرأة تزوجها بغير إذن مولاه، وكذا إذا أقر بجناية موجبة للمال لا يلزمه؛ لأن الإذن لم يتناول إلا التجارة، بخلاف ما إذا أقر بالحدود والقصاص؛ لأن العبد يبقى على أصل الحرية في حقهما. مكلف: شرط التكليف؛ لأن إقرار الصبي والمعتوه والمجنون لا يصح؛ لانعدام أهلية الإلزام، إلا إذا كان الصبي أو المعتوه مأذوناً له، فيصح إقراره بالمال؛ لكونه من ضرورات التجارة؛ لأنه لو لم يصح إقراره لا يعامله أحد، فدخل في الإذن كل ما كان طريقه التجارة كالديون والودائع والعواري والمضاربات والغصوب، فيصح إقراره
بها؛ لالتحاقه في حقها بالبالغ العاقل بخلاف ما ليس من باب التجارة كالمهر والجناية والكفالة، حيث لا يصح
Book of Iqrar. That is, in explanation of its rulings. It is a news of the establishment of a right of another on oneself when a free, adult sane person makes iqrar. That is, iqrar in Shari'ah: The Book of Iqrar: linguistically it means affirmation; it is said: qarra al-shay' when it becomes established, and aqarra ghayrahu when he establishes it [Ayni] derived from qarar, so linguistically it is an expression of establishing what was vacillating between denial and confirmation. In Shari'ah: it is a report, i.e. not a creation, and information by speech. So if he wrote or gestured and did not say anything, it is not iqrar, except if the one for whom the iqrar is made is absent and he wrote to him: 'To proceed, you have such-and-such on me,' then it is like speech. The condition of its validity is that the one making iqrar be an adult, sane, willingly. Being free is not a condition, so the iqrar of a slave is valid; it is executed immediately in what there is no suspicion (tuhma) such as hadd and qisas; in what there is suspicion he is not held accountable immediately because it is an iqrar against the master, but he is held accountable after emancipation due to removal of the impediment. Its pillar is a word or what is in its ruling indicating it. Its ruling is the appearance of the confessed right, not its initial establishment. Its evidence from the Book is His statement: 'And let the one upon whom the right is due dictate' (2:282) – He commanded him to dictate; if his iqrar were not accepted, his dictation would be meaningless. From the Sunnah: the stoning of Ma'iz and al-Ghamidiyyah due to their iqrar. Ijma': the community is agreed that iqrar is a proof against oneself. And rational: the sane person does not confess against himself falsely in what harms himself or his property, so the aspect of truthfulness outweighs in his own regard due to absence of suspicion and completeness of authority. (Fath) 'He is a report' etc., this is like the genus because it includes testimony, for it is also a report of the establishment of a right of another. That is, and this is the nature of a definition to include genus and differentia. He restricted it to being against himself: 'against himself' excludes because if it were against someone else for someone else it would be testimony, and for himself it would be a claim. He left the word 'right' unrestricted in 'establishment of a right' to include if the confessed right is of the type of removals like divorce and emancipation; for divorce is removal of the established tie by marriage, so if he confesses to divorce, a right is established for the woman that was not hers before. (Fath, Ayni) 'When a free person makes iqrar': he restricted it to make his iqrar absolutely valid, and avoided the slave, because the slave under interdiction has his iqrar regarding property deferred until after emancipation. Similarly, the authorized slave has his iqrar deferred regarding what is not a matter of trade, such as his iqrar for dower from intercourse with a woman he married without his master's permission. Similarly, if he confesses to an injury requiring monetary compensation, it is not binding because authorization only covers trade. Unlike if he confesses to hadd or qisas because the slave retains original freedom in those matters. 'Mukallaf': condition of responsibility because the iqrar of a child, an insane person, or a madman is invalid due to lack of capacity for obligation. Except if the child or insane person is authorized, then his iqrar regarding property is valid because it is necessary for trade; because if his iqrar were not valid, no one would deal with him. So authorization includes everything that is a matter of trade such as debts, deposits, loans, mudarabas, and usurpations, so his iqrar regarding them is valid because he becomes like an adult sane person in those rights. Unlike what is not a trade matter such as dower, injury, and guaranty, where it is not valid.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Iqrar (Confession) and is a definition_ruling lesson. The author first defines iqrar linguistically as 'establishment' (ithbat) and technically as a report (ikhbar) of a right of another upon oneself. This distinguishes it from creation (insha') and from testimony (shahada) which is a report about another's right on a third party. The conditions of validity are that the confessor be adult, sane, and free-willed; freedom is not a condition, so a slave's iqrar is valid but may be deferred in certain cases. The pillar (rukn) is a word or its equivalent. The ruling (hukm) is the appearance of the confessed right, not its initial creation. The proofs are from Quran (2:282), Sunnah (the hadith of Ma'iz and Ghamidiyyah), Ijma, and rational reasoning. The commentary then elaborates on the definition, noting that 'right' is left unrestricted to include removals like divorce and emancipation. The condition 'free' is explained to distinguish the slave's iqrar: his iqrar regarding property is deferred until after emancipation if he is under interdiction, or if authorized, deferred for non-trade matters. The condition 'mukallaf' excludes children, insane, and mad, unless they are authorized for trade, then their iqrar is valid for trade-related matters only.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A report (ikhbar) of the establishment of a right of another on oneself, as defined in this text.
حر مكلف (hurr mukallaf): A free person who is legally responsible (adult, sane, willing), as a condition for iqrar.
تهمة (tuhma): Suspicion of collusion or self-interest; iqrar by a slave in matters free from tuhma such as hadd/qisas is immediately effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the pillar (rukn) of iqrar according to the text?
A) Writing only
B) Gesture only
C) Utterance (lafz) or its equivalent
D) Silence

Key Takeaway
Iqrar in Hanafi law is a report that establishes a right against the confessor, valid with conditions of adult, sane, willing, and not requiring freedom.

Ambiguous Iqrar in Hanafi Fiqh
Discusses validity and conditions of ambiguous iqrar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ambiguous iqrar and explain when the confessor is compelled to clarify or believed with his oath.

Core Idea
An ambiguous iqrar is valid, but the confessor must clarify if the right can be specified.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position on an ambiguous confession (iqrar majhul) where the right claimed is unknown in amount or type. The source establishes that such an iqrar is valid and the confessor is compelled to explain what he meant, unless the ignorance is excessive. We also explore the intra-Hanafi difference regarding what counts as 'mal' (property) and the effect of the confessor's interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling on Ambiguous Iqrar
Read the first part of the passage: 'ولو مجهولا كشيء وحق، ويجب على بيانه'. This indicates that an iqrar for an unknown right is valid and the confessor must clarify it.
The Hanafi school permits ambiguity as long as clarification is possible.
2
Distinguish Between Types of Ignorance
The text contrasts ignorance of the right (allowed) with ignorance of the person (prohibited). For example, saying 'I owe one of us' is invalid. Similarly if the confessee is unspecified, the iqrar is not valid.
Excessive ignorance (tafahhush) renders the iqrar invalid.
3
Understand the Compulsion to Explain
The confessor is compelled to specify the right if it can be clarified. However, if he interprets the iqrar as a sale or lease, it is invalid because those contracts require exact specification.
He must explain what has value (ma lahu qima).
4
Examine Intra-Hanafi Differences on 'Mal'
There is a difference between al-Hidayah and al-Quduri regarding the minimum value for 'mal'. Al-Hidayah does not believe the confessor if he claims less than what is conventionally called mal, while al-Quduri accepts his word for any amount.
The text mentions that in Sharh al-Kafi, less than a dirham is not accepted by istihsan.

Source Text

صح: جواب "إذا" أي صح إقراره. (عيني) ولو مجهولا [ أي ولو كان الحق المقر به مجهولاً. (معدن)] لأن الحق قد يلزمه مجهولاً بأن يتلف مالاً لا يعرف قيمته أو يجرح جراحة لا يعرف أرشها أو يبقى عليه باقية حساب لا يعلم قدرها، وأما جهالة المقر فتضر كقوله: لك على أحدنا ألف؛ لجهالة المقضي عليه، ولو كان المقر له مجهولاً بأن قال: علي ألف لواحد من الناس لا يصح؛ لأنه لا يمكن جبره على البيان من غير تعيين المدعي، وذكر الناطفي في "واقعاته": أن الجهالة إذا تفحش لا يجوز، وإن لم يتفحش جاز بأن قال علي ألف لأحد هذين؛ لأن صاحب الحق لا يعدو من ذكره، وفي مثله يؤمر بالتذكير؛ لأنه قد ينسى صاحب الحق، ولا يجبر على البيان من غير تعيين المدعي. (عيني، فتح)
وحق بأن قال: علي لفلان شيء أو حق. (ط) ويجبر على بيانه: لأنه لزمه الخروج عما وجب عليه بالإقرار؛ لأن كثيراً من الأسباب تتحقق مع الجهالة كالغصب والوديعة؛ لأن الإنسان يغصب ما يصادف ويودع ما عنده من غير تحرير في قدره وجنسه ووصفه ولهذا يجبر عليه حتى لو فسر الإقرار بالبيع أو الإجارة لا يصح إقراره؛ لأن هذه العقود لا تصح مع الجهالة فلا يجبر على البيان. (عيني، فتح) ويبين ما له قيمة من ما يثبت في الذمة مكيلاً كان أو موزوناً أو عددياً، نحو: كر حنطة أو فلس أو جوزة، لا ما لا قيمة له كحبة حنطة وجلد ميتة وصبي حر، ولأنه رجوع عن الإقرار، فلا يصح. ولو قال في قوله: "علي حق": أردت به حق الإسلام لا يصدق؛ لأنه خلاف العرف وعليه التعويل، وينبغي أن يصدق في مثل حق الشفعة أو التطرق ونحوه (عيني فتح عناية وفي مال أي في ما إذا قال رجل: لفلان علي مال. (معدن) لم يصدق هذا الذي قاله صاحب الهداية"، وقال القدوري: يقبل قوله في القليل والكثير؛ لأن المال عبارة عما يتمول به، وذلك موجود في القليل والكثير، وفي شرح الكافي: إذا بين ما دون الدرهم نحو نصف درهم أو دائق، قالوا: القياس أن يصدق وفي الاستحسان أن لا يصدق في ما دون الدرهم. رمز الحقائق: ٢١٦/٢]
في أقل: لأن ما دونه من الكسور لا يطلق عليه اسم المال عادة. (عيني)
... with a right, it is valid, and even if unknown, like a thing or right, and he is compelled to explain it, and he explains what has value. And the saying belongs to the confessor with his oath if the confessee claims more than it. And in [the case of] 'mal' he is not believed in the minimum. In what is between... because he is the denier. ... his iqrar regarding them because trade is exchange of property with property, dower is exchange of property with non-property, injury is not exchange, and guaranty is a donation initially so it does not fall under authorization. The sleeping and the fainted are like the insane due to lack of discernment. The iqrar of the drunk is valid if he became drunk with a forbidden substance because it does not contradict address, except if he confessed what is revocable like pure hadd rights of Allah. If he became drunk with a permissible substance like forced drinking of wine, nothing is binding upon him. Similarly with drinking from grains or honey according to the two shaykhs, contrary to Muhammad. Apostasy is like pure hadd. (Fath) 'Sahha': answer to 'idha' meaning his iqrar is valid (Ayni). And even if unknown i.e. even if the confessed right is unknown (Ma'din). Because a right may become binding on him unknownly, such as destroying property of unknown value, or wounding with unknown compensation, or having a residual account of unknown amount. As for ignorance of the confessor, it is harmful, like if he says 'I owe one thousand to one of us' due to ignorance of the person owed. And if the confessee is unknown, like saying 'I owe one thousand to one of the people', it is not valid because he cannot be compelled to explain without specifying the claimant. Al-Natif said in his Waqi'at: ignorance if excessive is not permissible, if not excessive it is permissible, like saying 'I owe one thousand to one of these two' because the right holder is not beyond those mentioned. In such a case he is ordered to recall because he may have forgotten the right holder, but he is not compelled to explain without specification of the claimant. (Ayni, Fath) 'And 'right' if he said 'I owe to so-and-so a thing or right' (T). He is compelled to explain it because he has to fulfill what is due by iqrar. Because many causes occur with ignorance like usurpation and deposit; as a person usurps whatever he encounters and deposits what he has without specifying its measure, type, and description. Hence he is compelled. Even if he interprets the iqrar as a sale or lease, his iqrar is not valid because these contracts are not valid with ignorance, so he is not compelled to explain. (Ayni, Fath) And he explains what has value from what becomes established in the obligation, whether measured by volume, weight, or count, like a kurr of wheat, a fils, or a walnut; not what has no value like a grain of wheat, skin of a dead animal, or a free child. Because it would be a retraction from iqrar, so it is not valid. And if he said in his statement 'upon me a right' meaning 'I meant the right of Islam', he is not believed because it contradicts custom, and custom is relied upon. However, he should be believed in things like right of pre-emption or right of passage and the like. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah) 'And regarding mal', that is when a man says 'upon so-and-so I have mal' (Ma'din). He is not believed as the author of al-Hidayah said. Al-Quduri said his statement is accepted for little and much because 'mal' is an expression of what can be possessed, and that exists in little and much. In Sharh al-Kafi: if he specifies less than a dirham like half a dirham or a daniq, they said by qiyas he is believed but by istihsan he is not believed for less than a dirham. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:216) 'in the least' because less than that is not normally called 'mal'. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage continues from the previous lesson and deals with ambiguous iqrar. It is an expansion_probe type lesson. The text begins by stating that an iqrar with an unknown (majhul) right, like 'a thing' or 'a right', is valid, and the confessor is compelled to explain it. He must explain something that has value. The confessor is believed with his oath if the confessee claims more. Regarding the word 'mal' (property), the confessor is not believed if he claims the amount is less than what is conventionally called 'mal'. The text then returns to discuss the iqrar of a sleeping person, a faint person, and a drunk person, comparing them to majnun (insane). The drunk's iqrar is valid if he drank a forbidden substance because he is still capable of address. However, if what he confessed is revocable like pure hadd rights of Allah, it is not binding. If he drank something permissible like wine under coercion, his iqrar is not binding. The two shaykhs (Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) differ from Muhammad regarding drinks made from grains or honey. Apostasy is compared to pure hadd rights. The commentary then explains that the confession of an unknown right is valid because a right may become binding in an unknown state, e.g., destroying property of unknown value. Ignorance about the confessor or confessee can invalidate the iqrar if it is excessive. For example, saying 'I owe one thousand to one of us' is harmful due to ignorance of the person. If the confessee is unknown and not specified, the iqrar is invalid. However, if it is limited to a known group (e.g., 'one of these two'), it is valid and the confessor is ordered to recall. The text also discusses that if the confessor explains his ambiguous iqrar as a sale or lease, it is not valid because those contracts require specification. He must explain what has value. Finally, the text addresses the interpretation of 'right' and 'mal', and the difference of opinion between al-Hidayah and al-Quduri regarding what constitutes 'mal'.

Key Terms
مجهول (majhul): Unknown; refers to the confessed right being unspecified in amount or type.
مال (mal): Property; in the context of iqrar, it refers to something of value that can be traded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person says 'I owe someone a right' (علي حق) without specifying, what is the ruling?
A) The iqrar is invalid because it is too ambiguous.
B) The iqrar is valid and he is compelled to explain what he meant.
C) He is believed if he later says he meant 'right of Islam'.
D) The iqrar is valid only if the right is related to property.

Key Takeaway
An ambiguous iqrar is valid in Hanafi fiqh, but the confessor must clarify the unknown if possible, and his interpretation is subject to custom and legal limits.

Definition and General Ruling of Sulh (Settlement)
Defines sulh, states permissibility with iqrar and sukut, and lists conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of sulh, identify its validity with iqrar and sukut, and list its essential conditions and pillars.

Core Idea
Sulh is a contract that removes dispute, permissible with admission or silence, provided conditions are met.

Sulh (settlement) is a vital contract for resolving disputes in Islamic law. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq defines sulh, establishes its general permissibility based on a hadith, and outlines its pillars and conditions according to the Hanafi school. Understanding this foundation is crucial for properly applying sulh in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and scope of sulh
The passage defines sulh as a contract that removes dispute (يرفع النزاع). Note that it is a distinct contract in the Hanafi fiqh classification.
Sulh is derived from المصالحة (mutual reconciliation) and is the opposite of contention.
2
Determine the scenarios of valid sulh
The text states that sulh is permissible with iqrar (admission) and sukut (silence). Understand that Hanafis allow sulh even when the defendant neither admits nor denies.
This is a unique Hanafi position; Shafi'is require either admission or denial.
3
Extract the pillar (rukn) of sulh
The pillar is offer (إيجاب) and acceptance (قبول). This is the constitutive element without which the contract is invalid.
The text also notes that a mere request for sulh can suffice for acceptance if the claimed item is not specified.
4
List the conditions (shurut) for sulh
Conditions include: (a) the subject matter of the settlement (المصالح عليه) must be known if delivery is required; (b) the right being settled (المصالح عنه) must be a right for which compensation is permissible (e.g., property, qisas, but not preemption or hadd); (c) if the claim is specific, acceptance from the defendant is required; otherwise, a request suffices.
Ignorance (jahalah) that leads to further dispute invalidates the contract; but if it does not lead to dispute (e.g., mutual dropping of claims), it is allowed.
5
Analyze the dalil (proof) for permissibility
The hadith 'كل صلح جائز بين المسلمين إلا صلحاً أحل حراماً أو حرم حلالاً' (Reported by Abu Dawud) is cited. The general phrase indicates permissibility except when it alters halal/haram boundaries.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) and the evidence is general, but Hanafis rely on it for the principle of permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلح تعريف الصلح وأنواعه كتاب الصلح هو عقد يرفع النزاع وهو جائز بإقرار وسكوت أي الصلح في الشرع أي الصلح أي بإقرار المدعى عليه كتاب الصلح: اسم من المصالحة، وهو خلاف المخاصمة. ملا مسكين] مناسبة سابقه أن الصلح قد يكون عن إقرار، وقد يقال: وجه المناسبة: أن الإقرار يحصل به قطع المنازعة كما يحصل بالصلح. (فتح) قال في "البحر الرائق": وسببه سبب المعاملات تعلق المقدور بتعاطيه وركنه الإيجاب والقبول الموضوعان له، وشرطه: كون المصالح عليه معلوماً إن كان يحتاج إلى قبضه، والمصالح عنه حقا يجوز الاعتياض عنه ولو على غير مال كالقصاص معلوما كان أو مجهولا لا ما لا يجوز الاعتياض عنه كحق الشفعة وحد القذف والكفالة بالنفس، وطلب الصلح كاف عن القبول من المدعى عليه إن كان المدعى به مما لا يتعين بالتعين، وإن كان مما يتعين فلابد من قبول المدعى عليه ويشترط شرائط ذلك العقد الملحق به من بيع وإجارة. وحكمه في جانب المصالح عليه: وقوع الملك فيه للمدعي، سواء كان المدعى عليه مقرا أو منكرا وفي المصالح عنه: وقوع الملك فيه للمدعى عليه إن كان مما يحتمل التمليك كالمال وكان المدعى عليه مقرا به، وإن كان مما لا يحتمل التمليك كالقصاص وقوع البراءة، كما إذا كان منكرا مطلقاً، والجهالة فيه إن كانت تفضي إلى المنازعة كوقوعها فيما يحتاج إلى التسليم منعت صحته، وإلا لا، فبطل إن كان المصالح عليه أو عنه مجهولا يحتاج إلى التسليم كصلحه بعد دعواه مجهولا على أن يدفع له مالا ولم يسمه.

يرفع النزاع إلخ: أي بين المدعي والمدعى عليه، وركنه الإيجاب والقبول وشرطه: أن يكون البدل أي المصالح عليه مالاً معلوماً إن احتيج إلى قبضه وإلا لا يشترط معلوميته، فإن من ادعى حقاً في دار وادعى المدعى عليه قبله حقاً حانوته، فتصالحا أن يترك كل واحد منهما دعواه قبل صاحبه، صح، وإن لم يبين كل واحد منهما مقدار حقه؛ لأن جهالة ساقط لا يفضي إلى المنازعة. (شرح إلياس) بإقرار وسكوت إلخ [ أي لا يقر المدعى عليه ولا ينكر. (إلياس)] لإطلاق قوله عليه: "كل صلح جائز بين المسلمين إلا صلحاً أحل حراماً أو حرم حلالاً، أخرجه أبو داود
Book of Sulh: Definition of Sulh and its Types. The Book of Sulh: It is a contract that removes dispute, and it is permissible with admission and silence. That is sulh in the Sharia, i.e., with the admission of the defendant. The Book of Sulh: a noun from mutual reconciliation, and it is the opposite of contention. [Mulla Miskin]: The appropriateness of its preceding [book] is that sulh may be based on admission, and it is said: the aspect of appropriateness is that admission results in ending dispute just as sulh does. (Fath) He said in al-Bahr al-Raiq: Its cause is the cause of transactions - the connection of ability with its performance. Its pillar is offer and acceptance designated for it. Its condition: that the subject matter of sulh (al-musalah alayh) is known if it requires delivery, and the matter being settled (al-musalah anhu) is a right for which compensation is permissible, even if for other than property like qisas, whether known or unknown, not what compensation is not permissible for, like the right of preemption, the prescribed punishment for slander, and guarantee of person. And the request for sulh suffices for acceptance from the defendant if the claimed thing is not determined by specification, but if it is determined, acceptance of the defendant is necessary, and the conditions of that contract attached to it from sale and lease are required. Its ruling regarding the one making sulh (al-musalah alayh): ownership occurs for the plaintiff, whether the defendant is admitting or denying; regarding the matter being settled: ownership occurs for the defendant if it is something that permits ownership like property and the defendant admitted to it, but if it is something that does not permit ownership like qisas, then innocence (bara'ah) occurs, as when he is denier absolutely. And ignorance in it, if it leads to dispute like occurring in what requires delivery, prevents its validity, otherwise not. So it is invalid if the subject matter of sulh or the matter being settled is unknown and requires delivery, like his sulh after his claim of an unknown thing on condition that he pays him money without specifying it." "It removes dispute etc.": i.e., between the plaintiff and defendant. Its pillar is offer and acceptance, and its condition: that the substitute (al-badal), i.e., the subject matter of sulh, is known property if delivery is needed, otherwise knowledge is not required. For if someone claims a right in a house and the defendant claims a right in his shop, and they make sulh that each drop his claim against the other, it is valid, even if each does not specify the amount of his right, because ignorance of what is dropped does not lead to dispute. (Sharh Ilyas) "With admission and silence etc." [i.e., the defendant neither admits nor denies. (Ilyas)] due to the unrestricted nature of his statement, peace be upon him: "Every sulh is permissible among Muslims except a sulh that makes lawful what is unlawful or makes unlawful what is lawful," reported by Abu Dawud.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on the definition and general ruling of sulh (settlement) from the Hanafi school. The opening states that sulh is a contract (عقد) that removes dispute (يرفع النزاع) and is permissible (جائز) in two scenarios: with the defendant's admission (إقرار) or with his silence (سكوت), i.e., when he neither admits nor denies. The text then delves into the linguistic and technical definitions, noting that sulh is derived from المصالحة (mutual reconciliation) and is the opposite of contention (مخاصمة). The commentator (likely al-`Inayah or similar) provides the appropriateness of placing the Book of Sulh after the Book of Iqrar, as both end disputes. From the scholarly source al-Bahr al-Raiq, the passage details the cause (سبب), pillar (ركن), and conditions (شرط) of sulh: The cause is the connection of ability with performance; the pillar is offer (إيجاب) and acceptance (قبول); conditions include: the subject matter of the settlement (المصالح عليه) must be known if delivery is required, and the matter being settled (المصالح عنه) must be a right for which compensation is permissible (e.g., qisas is allowed even if unknown, but preemption and prescribed penalties are not). The ruling: For the settling party (المصالح عليه, the plaintiff), ownership of the substitute is established; for the matter being settled, ownership goes to the defendant if it is property and he admitted, or bara'ah (innocence) occurs if it is non-property like qisas. Ignorance that leads to dispute invalidates the contract, such as when the subject or consideration is unknown and requires delivery. The commentary then explains the phrase 'يرفع النزاع' and gives an example: if both parties have claims and they drop them mutually without specifying amounts, it is valid because ignorance of what is dropped does not lead to dispute. The proof for the permissibility of sulh with admission and silence is the hadith from Abu Dawud: 'Every sulh is permissible among Muslims except a sulh that makes lawful what is unlawful or makes unlawful what is lawful.' This hadith establishes the general principle of permissibility, with the exception of sulh that contradicts sharia rulings.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulh): A contract (عقد) that removes dispute (يرفع النزاع) between parties, permissible with admission or silence.
إقرار (iqrar): Admission by the defendant of the claim against him, which forms the basis for a valid sulh.
سكوت (sukut): Silence by the defendant, i.e., neither admitting nor denying the claim, which still allows the sulh to be valid per Hanafis.
عقد (aqd): A contract, defined by its pillar (offer and acceptance) and conditions.
جهالة (jahalah): Ignorance or uncertainty about the subject matter or substitute; invalidates sulh if it leads to dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling of sulh (settlement) when the defendant remains silent (sukut)?
A) It is not valid because silence is not consent.
B) It is valid based on the hadith 'Every sulh is permissible among Muslims'.
C) It is valid only if the defendant later admits.
D) It is invalid because it may lead to taking property unjustly.

Key Takeaway
Sulh is a contract that removes dispute, valid with admission or silence, provided the subject is known and the right is compensable, based on the general permission in the hadith.

Sulh by Acknowledgment as a Sale: Rulings and Conditions
States ruling on sulh by acknowledgment as sale with consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of sulh by acknowledgment when it involves property-for-property, identify its legal consequences (shuf'a, return due to defect, etc.), and determine the conditions that affect its validity (ignorance of badal vs. ignorance of musalah anhu).

Core Idea
Sulh by acknowledgment is treated as a sale, bringing all sale consequences, but ignorance of the substitute invalidates it.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains a unique Hanafi ruling: when a person acknowledges a debt or claim and then settles by exchanging property (e.g., giving a car in lieu of money), the settlement is legally considered a sale. This means all the normal sale rules apply—pre-emption, defect returns, options of sight and condition. But there's a catch: the settlement is only valid if the substitute (badal) is known; uncertainty about the original claim (musalah anhu) doesn't harm it. The passage also details what happens if a third party proves ownership of the property (istihqaq).

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type and its legal classification
The passage states 'sulh by acknowledgment with property for property' is considered a sale. Classify it as a sale contract.
This is the foundational classification.
2
List the specific sale consequences that apply
From the text: pre-emption (shuf'a), return due to defect (radd bi al-ayb), option of sight (khiyar al-ru'ya), and option of condition (khiyar al-shart).
These are automatic once the contract is valid.
3
Distinguish between valid and invalid forms based on ignorance
Ignorance of the substitute (badal) corrupts the sulh; ignorance of the matter compromised (musalah anhu) does not. This is a key condition.
The reason: the substitute is the consideration that must be delivered.
4
Analyze the effects of confiscation (istihqaq)
If the matter compromised is partly or wholly owned by a third party, the defendant recovers a proportional amount of the substitute. If the substitute is confiscated, the claimant recovers the original claim.
This ensures fairness when ownership is contested.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن وقع عن مال بمال بإقرار اعتبر بيعا، فيثبت فيه الشفعة والرد بالعيب وخيار الرؤية والشرط، وتفسده جهالة البدل لا جهالة المصالح عنه، وإن استحق بعض المصالح عنه أو كله، رجع المدعى عليه بحصة ذلك من العوض أو بكله، ولو استحق المصالح عليه أو وهو المصالح عن إقرار على المدعى بعضه رجع بكل المصالح عنه أو ببعضه.
If it occurs with property for property by acknowledgment, it is considered a sale. Thus, pre-emption, return due to defect, option of sight, and option of condition are established in it. Ignorance of the substitute corrupts it, not ignorance of the matter compromised. If some or all of the matter compromised is confiscated, the defendant recovers a proportional share of the compensation or all of it. And if the substitute is confiscated, or if it is the matter compromised by acknowledgment on the claimant, partially, he recovers all or part of the matter compromised.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage states a key ruling in Hanafi fiqh: when a person acknowledges a claim and then settles with the claimant by exchanging property for property (e.g., money for a house), the settlement (sulh) is treated as a sale contract. Consequently, all the subsidiary rulings of a sale apply: (1) pre-emption (shuf‘a) for neighbors in case of real estate, (2) the right to return the item if a defect is found, (3) the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru’ya) if the substitute was not seen at the time of contract, and (4) the option of stipulating a condition (khiyar al-shart). However, the settlement is corrupted (fasid) if there is ignorance (jahala) regarding the substitute (badal)—for example, if the substitute is an unspecified slave or animal—because such uncertainty leads to dispute. Ignorance of the matter being settled (al-musalah ‘anhu) does not corrupt it, as that matter is not required to be delivered. Furthermore, if it turns out that some or all of the claimed property (the matter compromised) is actually owned by a third party and is confiscated (istihqaq), the defendant who paid the substitute is entitled to recover from the claimant a proportional part of the substitute, or all of it. Conversely, if the substitute itself is confiscated, the claimant can recover the original claimed property from the defendant.

Key Terms
صلح عن إقرار (sulḥ ‘an iqrār): A settlement reached after the defendant acknowledges the claim, considered a sale if property is exchanged for property.
بدل (badal): The substitute or consideration given by the defendant in the settlement.
مصالح عنه (muṣāliḥ ‘anhu): The original claim or property that is being settled.
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): Confiscation due to a third party's proven ownership of the item in question.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, which element's ignorance (jahala) corrupts a sulh by acknowledgment?
A) The matter compromised (musalah anhu)
B) The substitute (badal)
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Sulh by acknowledgment with property for property is legally a sale, with all sale consequences; the substitute must be known or the contract is fasid, and istihqaq triggers mutual recovery of consideration.

Transgression in Deposits: Guarantee Does Not Cease Upon Removal
Commentary on the general rule and exceptions for guarantee after transgression

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why an amin's guarantee (daman) does not cease upon removing transgression, identify the exceptions, and state the mufta bihi regarding borrowers and lessees.

Core Idea
Transgression makes an amin liable; removing transgression does not erase liability except in ten cases.

A person entrusted with property (amin) who violates the terms of trust (transgression) immediately becomes liable for any loss. But what if he corrects his action and returns to compliance? Does the liability vanish? This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies that the guarantee (daman) persists, with only ten exceptions. It also resolves a key difference: borrowers and lessees are not treated like depositors in this regard. Let us walk through the reasoning and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
We have an amin (trustee) who transgresses (e.g., uses the deposit without permission) and then removes the transgression (returns it to its proper state). The question: does the guarantee (daman) that arose from the transgression cease?
The amin is any trustee: depositor, agent, partner, borrower, etc.
2
State the general principle
The commentary explains that the hand of an amin is legally like the hand of the owner, because the amin acts for the owner in preservation. This principle is the asl (root).
See al-Zayla'i's reasoning.
3
Apply the principle to the case
Because the hand is like the owner's, transgression breaks this trust and imposes a fresh liability. Removing the transgression does not restore the original immunity; the guarantee remains. The text says: 'لا يزول الضمان إلا في هذه العشرة'.
The exceptions are ten specific cases not fully enumerated here.
4
Note the key dispute
There is an intra-Hanafi disagreement: according to some (as in al-Durar), if a borrower or lessee transgresses and then returns to compliance within the original period, they become free of guarantee, like a depositor. But the mufta bihi is the opposite: borrower and lessee remain liable. The text guards against that minority view.
The author cites al-Shirunbulaliyya for the mufta bihi.
5
Practical consequence
The ruling protects the owner's rights. An amin cannot erase his liability simply by correcting his breach; he must fall within the ten exceptions or obtain explicit release from the owner.
This encourages trustees to avoid transgression from the outset.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التعدي في الوديعة وإقراره بعد جحوده، وله أن يسافر بها . عن أي وللمودع : بالوديعة هو عن بخلاف المودع؛ فإن يده يد المالك حكماً؛ لأنه عامل له في الحفظ، كذا في "الزيلعي"، ومثل المودع في البراءة الضمان الوكيل ببيع أو حفظ أو إجارة أو استئجار والمضارب والمستبضع وشريك عنان ومفاوضة ومستعير الرهن، والحاصل أن الأمين إذا تعدى، ثم أزاله لا يزول الضمان إلا في هذه العشرة؛ لأن يده كيد المالك، ولو كذبه في عوده إلى الوفاق، فالقول له وقيل للمودع، كذا في "الدر" "الأشباه" و"العمادية". واعلم أن ما ذكره المصنف من قوله بخلاف المستعير والمستأجر" المفتى به كما في "الشرنبلالية"، واحترز به عما ذكره في "الدرر" من " أن منهم من قال: المستعير والمستأجر كالمودع إذا خالفوا ثم عادوا إلى الوفاق، برءوا الضمان إذا كانت مدة الإيداع والإعارة باقية إلخ. ملا مسكين، فتح باختصار)
Transgression in the deposit, and his admission after his denial, and he may travel with it. Explanation: For the depositary: regarding the deposit, unlike the depositor; for his hand is the hand of the owner legally, because he is an agent for him in preservation, as in al-Zayla'i. And similar to the depositor in freedom from guarantee is the agent for sale, preservation, lease, hiring, the mudarib, the mustabdi', the partner of 'inan and mufawada, and the borrower of a pledge. The summary: when an amin transgresses and then removes it, the guarantee does not cease except in these ten; because his hand is like the hand of the owner. And if he is accused of lying in his return to compliance, the word is his, and it is said: for the depositor, as in al-Durr, al-Ashbah, and al-'Imadiyya. And know that what the author mentioned from his statement 'unlike the borrower and the lessee' is the mufta bihi as in al-Shirunbulaliyya, and he guarded thereby against what is mentioned in al-Durar that some said: the borrower and lessee are like the depositor when they violate and then return to compliance, they are freed from guarantee if the period of deposit and borrowing remains, etc. (Mulla Miskeen, Fath abbreviated).
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the ruling on transgression (ta'addi) in deposits. The commentary begins by explaining the general principle: the hand of an amin (trustee) is legally like the hand of the owner, because the amin is acting on behalf of the owner in preservation. Therefore, if an amin transgresses (e.g., uses the deposit without permission) and then removes the transgression (returns it to its proper state), the guarantee (daman) does not cease except in ten specific cases. The text lists several types of amins who are analogous: agent for sale, preservation, lease, hiring, mudarib, mustabdi', partner of 'inan and mufawada, and borrower of a pledge. The key point is that once guarantee attaches due to transgression, removing the transgression does not automatically nullify the guarantee unless the case falls within those ten exceptions. The commentary then notes that there is a difference of opinion regarding the depositor's word when the owner accuses him of lying about returning to compliance; according to some sources, the depositor's statement is accepted. Finally, it clarifies that the author's statement 'unlike the borrower and lessee' is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi), in contrast to a view in al-Durar that they are like the depositor. This shows the importance of distinguishing between different types of trustees.

Key Terms
أمين (amin): A trustee who holds property on behalf of another without liability for loss except in case of transgression.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for loss or damage to the deposit due to transgression.
يد المالك (yad al-malik): The legal hand of the owner; the amin's hand is considered like the owner's hand for purposes of preservation.
عشرة ('ashara): The ten specific cases where guarantee ceases even after transgression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what happens when an amin (trustee) transgresses and then removes the transgression?
A) The guarantee ceases immediately.
B) The guarantee does not cease except in ten specific cases.
C) The guarantee is transferred to the owner.
D) The guarantee doubles.

Key Takeaway
An amin's transgression creates an indelible liability; removing the transgression does not erase the guarantee unless the case falls within the ten exceptions.

Conditions for Liability upon Denial of a Deposit
Commentary on the ruling when the depositary denies and later admits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the six conditions required for the depositary's denial to create liability, and state the Hanafi ruling on real estate.

Core Idea
Denial after demand and moving the deposit dissolves the contract; liability persists even after admission.

In this lesson, we examine a detailed commentary on the phrase 'his admission after his denial' from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The text sets out precise conditions under which the depositary's denial triggers liability (daman) and why later admission does not remove it. Understanding these restrictions is essential to apply the Hanafi rule correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Read the opening statement: the depositary's admission after denial does not remove liability. The passage then explains why.
The ruling applies only when specific conditions are met.
2
Extract the underlying principle
The commentary states that denial lifts the contract (رفع للعقد), so the contract is rescinded and a new contract is needed for the deposit to be valid again.
This is analogous to denial by an agent or one of the contracting parties in a sale.
3
List the six restrictions (qayd)
Go through each 'قيدنا' (we restricted it) phrase in the text. There are six: (1) denial of the deposit itself (not claiming gift/sale); (2) after demand for return; (3) moving the deposit from its place; (4) property is movable; (5) no external threat (e.g., enemy); (6) depositary did not present the deposit after denial.
Each restriction is a condition that must be present for liability to attach.
4
Understand the consequence if a condition is missing
For each restriction, the text explains what happens if it is absent. For example, if the depositary claims the owner gifted it to him, there is no liability. If the deposit is real estate, no liability according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
This shows the conditional nature of the ruling.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Muhammad ibn al-Hasan holds that denial makes one liable even for real estate, but the more correct view (al-ashah) is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
This difference impacts practical application for property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإقراره بعد جحوده يعني أن المودع إذا جحد الوديعة بأن قال: لم يودعني مالكها بعد طلب ردها ونقلها من مكانها وقت الإنكار، وكانت منقولا، ولم يكن هناك من يخاف عليه منها، ولم يحضرها بعد الجحود لمالكها، ثم أقر بها لا يزول الضمان؛ لأن الجحود رفع للعقد، فيفسخ به العقد، فلا يعود إلا بعقد جديد كجحود الوكيل وجحود أحد المتعاقدين البيع. قيدنا بكونه أنكر الإيداع؛ لأن المودع لو ادعى أن المالك وهبها منه . أو باعها له وأنكر صاحبها، ثم هلكت لا ضمان على المودع، كذا في "الخلاصة"، وقيدنا بكونه بعد الطلب؛ لأنه لو قال له: "ما حال وديعتي عندك؟" ليشكر على حفظها، فجحدها لا ضمان عليه وقيدنا بكونه نقلها؛ لأنه لو لم ينقلها من مكانها حال جحوده، فهلكت لا ضمان عليه، وقيدنا بكونه منقولا؛ لأنها لو كانت عقارا لا يضمن بالجحود عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد في الأصح، ذكره الشارح في الغصب وقيدنا بكونه لم يكن من يخاف عليها منه؛ لأنه لو جحدها في وجه عدو، ويخاف عليها التلف إن أقر ثم هلكت لا يضمنها؛ لأنه إنما أراد حفظها، وقيدنا بكونه لم يحضرها؛ لأنه لو جحدها ثم أحضرها، فقال له صاحبها: دعها وديعة عندك فهلكت، فإن أمكنه أخذها فلم يأخذها لم يضمن؛ لأنه إيداع جديد، وإن لم يمكن ضمن؛ لأنه لم يتم الرد. [البحر الرائق: ٤٠٠/٧]
And his admission after his denial means that the depositary, if he denies the deposit by saying: 'He did not deposit it with me' after demand for its return and moving it from its place at the time of denial, and it was movable, and there was no one from whom he fears for it, and he did not present it after denial to its owner, then he admits it, the guarantee does not cease; because denial is a lifting of the contract, so the contract is rescinded by it, and it does not return except by a new contract, like the denial of the agent and the denial of one of the two contracting parties in sale. We restricted it to his denying the deposit; because if the depositary claimed that the owner gifted it to him or sold it to him and its owner denied, and then it perished, there is no guarantee on the depositary, as in al-Khulasa. And we restricted it to after demand; because if he said to him: 'What is the condition of my deposit with you?' to thank him for its preservation, and he denies it, there is no guarantee on him. And we restricted it to his moving it; because if he did not move it from its place during his denial, and it perished, there is no guarantee on him. And we restricted it to it being movable; because if it were real estate, he is not liable by denial according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad in the more correct view, as the commentator mentioned in the chapter on usurpation. And we restricted it to there being no one from whom he fears for it; because if he denies it in front of an enemy, and he fears its destruction if he admits, and then it perishes, he does not guarantee it; because he only intended its preservation. And we restricted it to his not presenting it; because if he denies it and then presents it, and its owner says: 'Leave it as a deposit with you,' and it perishes, if he was able to take it and did not take it, he does not guarantee; because it is a new deposit, and if he was not able, he guarantees; because the return was not completed. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 7/400]
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This is a detailed commentary on the matn phrase 'وإقراره بعد جحوده' (and his admission after his denial). The commentary explains the conditions under which the depositary's denial triggers liability (daman), and why subsequent admission does not remove that liability. The key principle is that denial dissolves the contract of deposit, similar to how denial by an agent or by one of the contracting parties in a sale rescinds the contract. Therefore, a new contract is needed for the deposit to be valid again. The commentary lists six restrictions (qayd) that must be present for liability to arise: (1) the depositary must deny the deposit itself (not claim ownership by gift or sale); (2) the denial must occur after the owner's demand for return; (3) the depositary must have moved the deposit from its place during denial; (4) the deposit must be movable (real estate is not subject to this rule according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf); (5) there must be no fear for the deposit from an external threat (e.g., an enemy); (6) the depositary must not have presented the deposit to the owner after denial. If any of these conditions are absent, the guarantee does not attach. This parsing exercise demonstrates the Hanafi method of setting precise conditions for legal liability.

Key Terms
جحود (juhud): Denial of the existence or receipt of the deposit, after demand for return.
قيد (qayd): A restrictive condition that limits the application of a ruling.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee or liability; here it means the depositary becomes liable for the value of the deposit.
عقد (aqd): Contract; here specifically the deposit contract (wadi'ah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for the depositary's guarantee upon denial?
A) The denial occurs after the owner's demand for return.
B) The deposit is movable property.
C) The depositary admits the deposit within one week.
D) The depositary moves the deposit from its place during denial.

Key Takeaway
The depositary's denial triggers liability only under six precise conditions; otherwise, it remains a trust.

The Depositary's Right to Travel with the Deposit: A Multi-School Analysis
Commentary on a matn statement examining conditions and multi-school dispute regarding depositary travel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the depositary traveling with the deposit, distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, and evaluate the Shafi'i/Maliki counter-argument.

Core Idea
The depositary may travel with the deposit unless prohibited or unsafe—but internal Hanafi differences and external condemnations reveal a rich legal debate.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses whether a depositary (the person entrusted with a deposit) may travel with the deposited item. The matn states 'and he may travel with it' but the commentary immediately qualifies this by introducing conditions and multiple scholarly opinions. Within the Hanafi school, Abu Hanifa grants absolute permission, while his students Abu Yusuf and Muhammad impose restrictions based on distance and weight. Outside the Hanafi school, al-Shafi'i and Malik prohibit travel entirely. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to trace how different usul principles yield different fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central question and matn statement
The matn says 'وله أن يسافر بها' (he may travel with it). This is the default ruling being commented on.
The commentary takes this as a starting point and then adds conditions.
2
List the conditions added by the commentary
The commentary specifies two conditions: no prohibition from the owner and no fear for the deposit (e.g., safe road).
These conditions are derived from the broader principles of amanah (trust) and preservation.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa: absolute permission (subject to conditions). Abu Yusuf: only short distances for heavy items. Muhammad: prohibits travel for heavy items entirely.
The difference hinges on the interpretation of 'mu'nah' (expense) and customary fear.
4
Contrast with external schools
Al-Shafi'i and Malik absolutely prohibit travel with a deposit, based on the argument that travel exposes property to destruction (the desert is destructive).
Their dalil is a rational argument (qiyas) linking travel to increased risk.
5
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Hanafi law, if conditions are met, the depositary is not liable for loss during travel. Under Shafi'i law, he is liable even without loss, because the act of traveling itself is a violation.
This practical difference affects whether compensation must be paid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله أن يسافر بها إلخ: أي وللمودع أن يسافر بها أي بالوديعة عند عدم النهي من صاحب الوديعة، وعند عدم الخوف عليها بالإخراج، وهذا على الإطلاق قول أبي حنيفة، وقال أبو يوسف له الخروج بها إلى مسافة قصيرة، وإن طالت لا يخرج فيما له حمل ومؤنة؛ لأنه يلزمه مؤنة الرد، ولأن القصيرة لا يخاف فيها عادة، وقال محمد الله: لا يخرج بما له حمل ومؤنة وقال الشافعي: ليس له أن يخرج مطلقاً، بها وبه قال مالك؛ لأنه عرض المال على الهلاك؛ لأن المفازة مهلكة، ولأبي حنيفة له: أنه أمره بالحفظ مطلقاً، وقد أتى به، وما ذكره من المؤنة من ضرورات حفظ ماله، فلا يأباه، ولو كان الطريق مخيفاً ليس له أن يسافر بها إن كان له منه بد، وإن لم يكن له منه بد وسافر مع أهله لا يضمن ولو نهاه أن يخرج بها من المصر، فخرج بها ضمن إن كان له منه بد، وإن لم يكن له منه بد لا يضمن، وأجمعوا على أنه لو سافر بها في البحر يضمن قاله الإسبيجابي ليه، وقال قاضي خان في "شرح الجامع الصغير": وأجمعوا على أن الأب والوصي إذا سافر بمال الابن أو اليتيم لا يضمن، والوكيل بالبيع إذا سافر بما وكله بالبيع، قالوا: إن قيده بمكان بأن قال له بعه بالكوفة، فسافر به يضمن، وإن أطلق إطلاقاً، فسافر به لا يضمن إذا سرق أوضاع فيما لا حمل له ويضمن فيما له حمل ومؤونة. (عيني) عند عدم النهي أي فهي المالك عن المسافرة بالوديعة. (ملا مسكين عيني والخوف: أي له أن يسافر بها إذا لم يكن الطريق مخوفاً، ومن المخوف السفر بها في البحر ؛ لأن الغالب فيه العطب، كذا في "البحر" [٤٠١/٧] "الاختيار" وتعقبه الحموي نقلاً عن المقدسي، فقال: يخالف هذا الإطلاق قولهم في الحج: يجب إذا كان الغالب السلامة، ولو بحراً، انتهى، وأجيب بأن التقييد مستفاد من تعليله. (فتح)
And he may travel with it etc.: i.e., the depositary may travel with it, i.e., with the deposit, in the absence of prohibition from the owner of the deposit, and in the absence of fear for it by taking it out. And this is, absolutely, the saying of Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: He may take it out to a short distance, but if long, he should not take it out if it has weight and burden; because the expense of return is incumbent on him, and because in short distances fear is not customary. Muhammad said: He should not take out what has weight and burden. Al-Shafi'i said: He may not take it out at all, and Malik said the same; because it exposes the property to destruction, as the desert is destructive. Abu Hanifa's proof: He ordered him to preserve it absolutely, and he has fulfilled that. And what is mentioned about expense is from the necessities of preserving his property, so he does not object. And if the road is frightening, he may not travel with it if he has an alternative; if he has no alternative and travels with his family, he does not guarantee. And if he prohibited him from taking it out of the city, and he took it out, he guarantees if he has an alternative; if he has no alternative, he does not guarantee. And they are unanimous that if he travels with it by sea, he guarantees; al-Isbijabi said this. Qadi Khan said in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: They are unanimous that the father and executor, when they travel with the property of the son or orphan, do not guarantee. And the agent for sale, if he travels with what he was authorized to sell: if he restricted it to a place by saying 'sell it in Kufa,' and he travels with it, he guarantees; if he gave absolute permission, and he travels with it, he does not guarantee if it is stolen or lost in what has no burden, but he guarantees in what has weight and burden. ('Ayni) 'in the absence of prohibition': i.e., the owner's prohibition against traveling with the deposit. (Mulla Miskeen and 'Ayni: 'and fear': i.e., he may travel with it if the road is not frightening; among frightening things is traveling with it by sea because destruction is predominant, as in al-Bahr [7/401] and al-Ikhtiyar. Al-Hamawi objected by quoting al-Maqdisi, saying: This absolute statement contradicts their statement in Hajj: it becomes obligatory if safety is predominant, even by sea. He ended, and the answer was that the restriction is derived from its justification. (Fath)
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage discusses the depositary's right to travel with the deposit. The matn states 'وله أن يسافر بها' (he may travel with it), and the commentary explains the conditions and differing opinions. Abu Hanifa gives absolute permission as long as there is no explicit prohibition from the owner and no fear for the deposit's safety. Abu Yusuf restricts this to short distances for items with weight and burden, due to the cost of return and lack of customary fear. Muhammad agrees with Abu Yusuf on not taking heavy items. Al-Shafi'i and Malik prohibit travel altogether, arguing that it exposes the property to destruction. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the owner ordered preservation absolutely, and traveling can be a means of preservation; any expense incurred is a natural consequence. However, exceptions are noted: if the road is dangerous, travel is not permitted unless unavoidable; if the owner explicitly prohibits leaving the city, and the depositary does so despite having an alternative, he is liable. Unanimously, travel by sea incurs liability due to high risk. The commentary also cites parallels: a father or guardian traveling with a child's property is not liable; an agent for sale may travel with the goods if not restricted by location, but is liable if the goods have weight and burden unless absolute permission is given. This illustrates the Hanafi method of balancing absolute permission with practical restrictions, and the use of istihsan (equity) in deriving rulings.

Key Terms
مؤنة (mu'nah): Expense or burden, especially the cost of returning the deposit from a distant location.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the default ruling regarding the depositary traveling with the deposit?
A) He may travel with it only if the owner explicitly permits.
B) He may travel with it absolutely as long as there is no prohibition or fear.
C) He may travel with it only to short distances.
D) He may never travel with it.

Key Takeaway
The depositary's right to travel with the deposit is conditional on no prohibition and no fear, and even within the Hanafi school, there is a spectrum of views ranging from absolute permission to heavy restrictions.

Joint Deposits: Giving One Share Without the Others
Ruling on joint deposit and delivery of one depositor's share

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving one joint depositor his share without the others and explain the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
For fungibles, Abu Hanifa forbids giving a share until all depositors are present; the Sahibayn permit it.

Joint deposits, where two or more persons entrust a fungible item to a single trustee, raise a practical question: if one depositor appears alone and demands his share, may the trustee give it? The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states a clear prohibition, but the Sahibayn disagree. This lesson walks through the ruling, its limits, and the dispute's practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The act is the trustee (mudi') delivering a part of a joint deposit to one of the depositors when the others are absent. Confirm that the deposit is a fungible (mithli) item like grain or oil; the ruling differs for non-fungibles.
2
State the default ruling (Abu Hanifa)
According to Abu Hanifa – and this is the position in the matn – the depositary must not give until all depositors are present. This is supported by a narration from Caliph 'Ali.
The matn says 'lam yudfa' al-mudi' ila ahadihima hazzahu hatta yahdura al-akhar'.
3
State the dissent (Sahibayn) and their dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the depositary may give, because the depositor is claiming his own property, just as if all were present. The ruling applies only to fungible goods.
The phrase 'wa qala: lahu dhalik' refers to the Sahibayn.
4
Analyse the consequences
If the depositary gives contrary to Abu Hanifa's view, the act is not a valid division (qismah); if the remainder perishes, the other depositor can recover from the taker. Also, regarding liability, if the depositary gives despite a prohibition, Abu Hanifa said he is not liable (since the loss is not from his act), but in a variant where depositors explicitly forbade giving, Muhammad said he is liable by qiyas, though Abu Yusuf excused him by istihsan.
The commentary elaborates these nuances.
5
Determine the operative position (mufta bihi)
Later scholars dispute which view is relied upon. Al-Nasafi, al-Mahbubi, and Sadr al-Shari'a preferred Abu Hanifa; Qadi Khan's Fatawa and al-Bahr consider Abu Yusuf's view as chosen due to istihsan. The student should note that both positions have textual support, and practice may vary.
The tarjih is not settled; mufti may choose based on context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اودعا إلخ: أي ولو أودع رجلان شيئا عند رجل مما يكال أو يوزن، فحضر أحدهما يطلب نصيبه، لم يدفع إليه، وهو معنى قوله : لم يدفع المودع بفتح الدال إلى أحدهما أي أحد الاثنين حظه أي نصيبه حتى يحضر الآخر - أبي حنيفة له، وهو مروي عن علي له، وقالا : له ذلك؛ لأنه طلب نصيبه كما لو حضرا، وبه الثلاثة، وإن كانت الوديعة من غير ذوات الأمثال ليس له ذلك بالإجماع. رمز الحقائق: ٢٥٣/٢، ٢٥٤] لم يدفع المودع إلخ فيه إشارة إلى أنه لا يجوز له الدفع، حتى لو خاصمه إلى القاضي لم يأمره بدفع نصيبه إليه في قول أبي حنيفة، وإلى أنه لو دفع إليه لا يكون قسمة، حتى إذا هلك الباقي رجع صاحبه على الآخذ، وإلى أن لأحدهما أن يأخذ حصته منها إذا ظفر بها، وإلى أنه لو دفع وارتكب الممنوع لا يضمن. وفي "فتاوى قاضي خان ما يفيده ولفظه: ثلاثة أودعوا رجلا، وقالوا لا تدفع المال إلى أحد منا حتى نجتمع، فدفع نصيب أحدهم، قال محمد: في القياس يضمن، وبه قال أبو حنيفة، وفي الاستحسان لا يضمن، وهو قول أبي يوسف، فقد جعل عدم الضمان هو الاستحسان، فكان هو المختار ، كذا في "البحر" [٤٠٢/٧] وتعقبه المقدسي فقال: كيف يكون هو المختار مع أن سائر المتون على قول الإمام، وقال الشيخ قاسم: اختار النسفي قول الإمام والمحبوبي وصدر الشريعة، وقال المقدسي: وقول بعضهم عدم الضمان هو المختار مستدلا بكون الاستحسان مخالفا لما عليه الأئمة الأعيان، بل غالب المتون عليه ،متفقون كذا في "الحموي". (فتح المعين) يحضر الآخر إلخ عند أبي حنيفة، ولو فعل ضمن نصفه وعندهما يدفع إليه نصيبه، ولا يضمن، والخلاف في المكيل والموزون، وفي "الذخيرة" ذكر الخلاف في الثياب والدواب أيضاً، وكذا في "الكافي"، والصحيح أن الخلاف فيما هو من ذوات الأمثال كالمكيلات والموزونات وفيما عداهما من الثياب والدواب والعبيد، فليس للحاضر أن يأخذ نصيبه بالإجماع. (ملا مسكين)
And if two deposit etc.: i.e., if two men deposit something with a man that is measured or weighed, and one of them comes asking for his share, he is not given to him. And this is the meaning of his statement: the depositary does not give to one of them his share until the other comes - according to Abu Hanifa, and it is narrated from 'Ali. And they two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he may do that, because he asks for his share just as if both had come; and with them are the three. And if the deposit is not of fungible things, he may not do that by consensus. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/253-254] 'The depositary does not give' etc.: there is an indication that he may not give, so that if he sues him before the judge, the judge does not order him to give his share to him according to Abu Hanifa; and that if he gives to him, it is not a division, so that if the remainder perishes, the other owner recovers from the taker; and that one of them may take his share from it if he gets hold of it; and that if he gives and commits the prohibited, he does not guarantee. In Fatawa Qadi Khan, its purport: Three persons deposited with a man and said, 'Do not give the money to any of us until we all meet.' He gave the share of one of them. Muhammad said: by analogy he guarantees, and Abu Hanifa agreed; by istihsan he does not guarantee, which is the saying of Abu Yusuf. He made non-liability the istihsan, so it is the chosen one. As in al-Bahr [7/402]. Al-Maqdisi objected, saying: How can it be the chosen one when all other texts follow the Imam's saying? Shaykh Qasim said: al-Nasafi chose the Imam's saying, and al-Mahbubi and Sadr al-Shari'a. Al-Maqdisi said: The saying of some that non-liability is chosen, using as proof that istihsan contradicts the great imams, rather most texts agree on it, as in al-Hamawi. (Fath al-Mu'in) 'until the other comes' etc. according to Abu Hanifa; if he does so, he guarantees half of it; according to them two, he gives his share and does not guarantee. The dispute is in measured and weighed items. In al-Dhakhira, the dispute is also mentioned for clothes and animals; similarly in al-Kafi. The correct view is that the dispute is in fungible things like measured and weighed items; for other things like clothes, animals, and slaves, the present one may not take his share by consensus. (Mulla Miskeen)
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage deals with joint deposits, where two or more persons deposit a fungible item (like grain or oil) with one trustee, and one of them comes to demand his share. The matn states 'لم يدفع المودع إلى أحدهما حظه حتى يحضر الآخر' (the depositary shall not give one of them his share until the other comes). The commentary explains this is the position of Abu Hanifa, which is also narrated from Caliph 'Ali. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, holding that the depositary may give the share because the depositor is asking for his own property, just as if both were present. The dispute is limited to fungible goods (mithliyyat); for non-fungibles, there is consensus that the depositary must not give. The commentary elaborates on the consequences: if the depositary gives despite the prohibition, it is not a valid division, so if the remaining share perishes, the other depositor can claim from the one who took his share. Additionally, if the depositary gives and the item perishes, he is not liable for guarantee according to Abu Hanifa (since he acted wrongly but the loss was not due to his act). However, in a variant case where three depositors instructed the trustee not to give until all are present, Muhammad says the trustee is liable by qiyas, but Abu Yusuf says he is not liable by istihsan. The commentary reports a disagreement among later scholars about which view is mufta bihi: some prefer Abu Hanifa's view, others prefer Abu Yusuf's. The final note clarifies that the dispute only applies to fungibles; for non-fungibles, consensus prohibits giving without the others.

Key Terms
ذوات الأمثال (dhawat al-amthal): Fungible goods that can be replaced by similar items, such as measured or weighed goods.
قسمة (qismah): A formal division of jointly owned property; here, giving one depositor his share without the others does not constitute a valid division.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if two persons deposit a fungible item (e.g., grain) with a trustee, and one of them comes to demand his share, what should the trustee do?
A) Give him his share immediately.
B) Not give until the other depositor is present.
C) Divide the deposit in the presence of a judge.
D) Refuse and keep the deposit until the owner dies.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal ikhtilaf on delivering a joint depositor's share of fungible goods: Abu Hanifa prohibits it until all are present, while Sahibayn permit it; later tarjih is unsettled.

Invalidity of Exception and Condition in Gift
Ruling on istithna and shart in hiba with comparisons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why an exception (istithna') or condition (shart) attached to a gift (hiba) is void (batil) in Hanafi fiqh, and distinguish between contracts where exception is valid or invalid.

Core Idea
Exception and condition in a gift are void because the gift contract does not operate on descriptions.

In Hanafi law, a gift is a unilateral transfer of ownership without compensation. When a donor tries to exclude something from the gift or attach a condition, the default ruling is that the exception or condition is invalid. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the reasoning and contrasts with financial exchanges and bequests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The matn states: 'وبطل الاستثناء والشرط' — the exception and condition become invalid. This means the exception/condition is void, but the gift itself remains valid unless the condition fundamentally changes the contract.
Do not confuse with voiding the entire gift.
2
Understand the rationale
The exception is invalid because 'it only operates where the contract operates'. A gift contract operates on the entire subject as a whole, not on partial descriptions. For example, a fetus is a description attached to the mother, not an independent owned entity, so excluding it is ineffective.
The contract cannot partially exclude something that is not separately owned.
3
Compare with other contracts
Financial exchanges (sale, lease) allow exceptions because the prohibition is against sale with a condition, not exception. Bequests (wasiyya) also allow exceptions because 'their scope is wide'. The same ruling applies to all non-financial exchanges (marriage, khul', etc.).
The distinction is based on the nature of the contract.
4
Apply to cases
Case: 'I gift you this slave except her pregnancy' → exception void, gift includes the slave and fetus. Case: if the fetus is mudabbar (promised freed), the gift is invalid because the donor still owns the fetus, so the gift is of an 'occupied' item.
Ownership of the excepted part matters.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school permits exception in gifts, whereas Hanafis do not. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
Respect scholarly differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبطل الاستثناء والشرط، ومن قال لمديونه : إذا جاء غد فهو لك، أو أنت بريء منه، في قوله إلا حملها في سائر الصور أي الدين أو إن أديت إلى نصفه فلك نصفه، أو أنت بريء من النصف الباقي، فهو باطل. أي نصف الدين الدين وبطل الاستثناء: لأنه لا يعمل إلا في محل يعمل فيه العقد، والهبة لا تعمل في الحمل؛ لكونه وصفا، وكذا الحكم في كل معاوضة مال بغير مال كالنكاح والخلع والصلح عن دم عمد والصدقة ،والعتق، بخلاف المعاوضات المالية كالبيع والإجارة والرهن والكتابة؛ لأنه عليها نهى عن بيع وشرط، وبخلاف الوصية، حيث تجوز في الأم دون الحمل، وفي الحمل دون الأم؛ لأن بابها واسع ولو أعتق حملها ثم وهبها صح؛ لأن الجنين غير مملوك له، فاشتغال بطنها به لا يوجب الفساد، بخلاف ما إذا دبر الحمل ثم وهبها حيث لا تجوز الهبة؛ لأن ملكه فيه باق، فكانت هبة مشغول بخلاف الأول. (زيلعي، بحر، فتح)
And the exception and the condition become invalid. And whoever says to his debtor: 'When tomorrow comes, it is yours' or 'you are free from it' — in his saying 'except her pregnancy' in all other cases — meaning the debt — or 'if you give me half of it, then you have half of it' or 'you are free from the remaining half' — it is invalid. Meaning half of the debt. And the exception becomes invalid because it only operates in a place where the contract operates, and the gift does not operate on the pregnancy because it is a description. And the same ruling applies to every exchange of property for non-property, like marriage, khul', reconciliation for intentional homicide, charity, and manumission. Contrary to financial exchanges like sale, lease, pledge, and kitabah, because there is a prohibition against a sale and a condition. And contrary to a bequest, where it is valid [to bequeath] to the mother without the fetus, and to the fetus without the mother, because its scope is wide. And if he manumitted her pregnancy then gifted her, it is valid because the fetus is not owned by him, so its occupation of her womb does not necessitate invalidity. Contrary to if he made the fetus a mudabbar then gifted her, where the gift is not valid because his ownership over it remains, so it was a gift of something occupied, unlike the first. (Zayla'i, Bahr, Fath).
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq with commentary expansion. The ruling: an exception (istithna') and a condition (shart) in a gift (hiba) are invalid. The exception is invalid because a gift does not operate on something that is merely a description, like the fetus in a pregnant slave. The same applies to any exchange of property for non-property (e.g., marriage, khul', etc.) because the underlying contract does not operate on descriptions. However, financial exchanges like sale and lease allow exceptions because the prohibition is against selling with a condition. Bequests are broader and can include or exclude the fetus. If a manumits the fetus and then gifts the mother, it is valid because the fetus is not owned. But if he makes the fetus a mudabbar (promised to be freed after his death) and then gifts the mother, the gift is invalid because he still owns the fetus, making the gift of a 'occupied' item.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): Excluding something from the subject of a gift, e.g., 'I gift you this slave except his hand.'
الشرط (al-shart): A condition attached to the gift, e.g., 'on condition that you do X.'
هبة (hiba): Gift — transfer of ownership without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the gift contract does not operate on a description like the fetus.
B) Because exceptions are only allowed in wills.
C) Because the gift is a financial exchange.
D) Because the Prophet prohibited all exceptions.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, any exception or condition in a gift is void, but the gift itself remains valid, covering the entire subject.
