Conditional Release from Debt: ta'liq vs taqyid
Detailed ruling on conditional ibra' with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between invalid ta'liq and valid taqyid in ibra' and apply the Hanafi ruling to conditional release scenarios.

Core Idea
Ta'liq invalidates ibra'; taqyid does not — the key is the word 'if' versus 'on condition that'.

You might think that any condition attached to releasing a debt is invalid. But the Hanafi school makes a sharp distinction: suspending the release on a future event (ta'liq) is void because ibra' is partly ownership, while restricting the release with a concurrent condition (taqyid) is perfectly valid. This lesson unpacks that distinction using the detailed commentary in our source text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the invalid statements
List the conditional phrases given in the passage (e.g., 'If you come tomorrow, you are free', 'If you pay half, you have half'). Note that all are declared batil (invalid).
2
Understand the nature of ibra'
Ibra' is both tamlik (ownership) and isqat (release). Because it gives ownership to the debtor in one respect, it cannot be treated like pure releases (divorce, manumission) which are used in oaths and can be suspended.
3
Contrast ta'liq and taqyid
Ta'liq makes the release contingent on a future event (using 'if' or 'when'). Taqyid restricts the scope but is not contingent (using 'on condition that'). The passage gives the example: 'You are free from half on condition that you pay me half' – this is valid because it is taqyid, not ta'liq.
The difference is subtle but crucial: ta'liq suspends the effect, taqyid describes the terms.
4
Apply the rule to new examples
Given new scenarios, classify whether the phrasing is ta'liq (invalid) or taqyid (valid). For instance, 'I release you provided you pay by Monday' – is that ta'liq or taqyid? (It is ta'liq because 'provided' implies conditionality, so invalid.)

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشرط: أي وبطل الشرط في سائر الصور؛ لأنه بعض أو مجهول والهبة لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة. (در، زيلعي) قال الحموي وقوله: "في سائر الصور أي في جميع الصور، ولا يصح أن يكون "سائر" هنا بمعنى باقي، يعني لو أريد سائر بمعنى باقي يلزم صحة الشرط في بعض الصور، وأقول: ذكر العيني مانصه وبطل الشرط في الصور الباقية، ولا مانع من صحته؛ لأنه يراد به باقي الصور ما عدا الأول من صورة الاستثناء رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٦/٢] ومن قال إلخ: أي من قال لمديونه : إذا جاء غد فهو أي الدين لك، أو أنت بريء منه أي من الدين، أو قال للمديون: إن أديت إلي نصفه أي نصف الدين فلك نصفه، أو قال: إن أديت إلى نصفه فأنت بريء من الباقي، فهو أي قوله هذا كله باطل؛ لأن الإبراء تمليك من وجه وإسقاط من ،وجه وهبة الدين ممن عليه الدين إبراء إلا أنه تمليك من وجه ولهذا يرتد بالرد ولا يتوقف بالقبول والتعليق بالشروط يختص بالإسقاطات المحضة التي يحلف بها كالطلاق والعتاق وهذا تمليك من وجه فلا يجوز تعليقه بالشرط، فتبطل بخلاف قوله: أنت النصف على أن تؤدي إلي النصف غدا؛ لأنه تقييد وليس بتعليق رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٦/٢] إن أديت إلخ: قيد بقوله: "إن أديت"؛ لأنه لو قال: أنت بريء عن النصف على أن تؤدي إلي النصف صح؛ لأنه ليس بتعليق بل تقييد؛ لأن المعلق بـ"علي" هو بعدها لا ما قبلها. [البحر الرائق: ٤٣٠/٧] (فتح) فهو باطل أي كل واحد من هذه الأقاويل باطل؛ لأن هبة الدين ممن عليه الدين إبراء، وهو تمليك من وجه، فيرتد بالرد، ولو بعد المجلس على خلاف فيه، وإسقاط من وجه، فلا يتوقف على القبول، إلا إذا أوجب انفساخ عقد صرف أو سلم والتعليق يختص بالإسقاطات المحضة التي يحلف بها كالطلاق والعتاق، فلا يصح تعليق التمليكات، ولا الإسقاطات من وجه أو من كل وجه مما لا يحلف به كالعفو عن القصاص، وفي قولهم: "والتعليق يختص بالإسقاطات المحضة التي يحلف بها" إشارة إلى أن من الإسقاطات المحضة ما لا يحلف بها أي لا يقبل التعليق بالشرط كالحجر على المأذون وعزل الوكيل والإبراء عن الدين. (فتح)
And the condition: meaning that the condition becomes invalid in all other cases, because it is partial or unknown, and the gift is not invalidated by invalid conditions. (Durr, Zayla'i). Al-Hamawi said: 'His saying "in all other cases" means in all cases, and it is not correct that "all" here means remaining, meaning if 'all' were intended as remaining, it would imply the validity of the condition in some cases.' I say: Al-'Ayni mentioned: 'And the condition is invalid in the remaining cases, and there is no objection to its validity because it is intended for the remaining cases except the first case of exception.' (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:266). 'And whoever says etc.': meaning whoever says to his debtor: 'When tomorrow comes, it (the debt) is yours' or 'you are free from it (the debt),' or says to the debtor: 'If you pay me half of it (half of the debt), then you have half of it,' or: 'If you pay me half of it, then you are free from the remaining,' then all of this statement is invalid because ibra' (release) is an ownership in one respect and a release in another. And the gift of a debt from the debtor is an ibra', except that it is an ownership in one respect, and therefore it is returned by rejection and does not require acceptance. And suspension (ta'liq) by conditions is specific to pure releases that are sworn by, like divorce and manumission. This is an ownership in one respect, so its suspension by a condition is not allowed, so it becomes invalid. Unlike his saying: 'You have half on condition that you pay me half tomorrow' because it is a restriction (taqyid) and not a suspension (ta'liq). (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:266). 'If you pay etc.': He restricted with his saying 'if you pay' because if he said: 'You are free from half on condition that you pay me half,' it is valid because it is not a suspension but a restriction, because what is attached by 'on condition' is what comes after it, not what comes before. (Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7:430). (Fath). So it is invalid: i.e., each one of these statements is invalid because the gift of a debt from the debtor is ibra', and it is an ownership in one respect, so it is returned by rejection, even after the session according to a difference of opinion, and a release in one respect, so it does not depend on acceptance, unless it necessitates the cancellation of a currency or salam contract. And suspension is specific to pure releases that are sworn by, like divorce and manumission. So it is not valid to suspend ownerships, nor releases in one respect or all respects that are not sworn by, like the waiver of retaliation. In their saying: 'Suspension is specific to pure releases that are sworn by,' there is an indication that among pure releases there are those that are not sworn by, i.e., they do not accept suspension by a condition, like interdiction upon a permitted slave, dismissal of an agent, and release from debt. (Fath).
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the ruling regarding conditional release (ibra') from debt. The matn states that all such conditional statements (e.g., 'If you pay me half, you are free from the rest') are invalid. The commentary explains that ibra' is both a form of ownership (because the debtor acquires a benefit) and a release (surrender of right). Suspension (ta'liq) is only valid for pure releases that are used in oaths, like divorce and manumission. Since ibra' has an element of ownership, it cannot be suspended. However, if the condition is phrased as a restriction (taqyid) using 'on condition that' (على) instead of 'if' (إن), it is valid. The key distinction: ta'liq makes the thing contingent on a future event, while taqyid simply restricts the scope of the release. Thus, 'You are free from half on condition that you pay me half' is valid because it is not suspending but restricting.

Key Terms
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Release from a debt — the creditor surrenders the right to demand payment.
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspending a legal consequence on the occurrence of a future condition, e.g., 'If you come, then you are free.'
التقييد (al-taqyid): Restricting the scope of a statement without making it contingent, e.g., 'on condition that you pay.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following statements regarding release from debt is valid in the Hanafi school?
A) 'If you come tomorrow, you are free from the debt.'
B) 'You are free from half on condition that you pay me half.'
C) 'When next month arrives, your debt is forgiven.'
D) 'If you pay me, I release you.'

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a conditional release using 'if' or 'when' is invalid because ibra' is partly ownership; but a restriction using 'on condition that' is valid.

Validity of 'Umra and Invalidity of Ruqba
Ruling with proof on 'umra and ruqba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'umra (valid) and ruqba (invalid) in Hanafi fiqh, citing the hadith and explaining the rationale.

Core Idea
'Umra is valid and passes to heirs; ruqba is invalid due to ambiguity (ta'liq).

This passage clarifies two types of conditional gifts: 'umra (life grant) and ruqba (each awaiting the other's death). The Hanafi school holds 'umra valid based on explicit sunnah, while ruqba is invalid according to the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) because the transfer is not immediate. We will explore the proofs and the intra-madhhab dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two transactions
Define 'umra (life grant) and ruqba (conditional transfer) as described in the passage. Note the key difference: 'umra grants immediate ownership for life; ruqba suspends transfer on death of either party.
In 'umra, the property is given for the recipient's lifetime and then to his heirs. In ruqba, ownership is conditional on who dies first.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, 'umra is valid (sahih) and ruqba is invalid (batil). The passage explains that the condition of return in 'umra is abrogated but does not invalidate the gift. Ruqba is invalid because ownership is not immediate.
Abu Yusuf differs on ruqba, considering it valid if interpreted as immediate transfer.
3
Analyze the hadith proof for 'umra
The hadith 'Whoever grants a life grant, it is for the recipient in his life and death' is cited. It proves that ownership passes to the recipient and his heirs, and any condition of return is void.
The hadith is reported by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and Nasa'i. It is ahad but clear in meaning (dalala qati).
4
Examine the dispute over ruqba
The majority (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad) hold ruqba invalid due to lack of immediate ownership. Abu Yusuf and others cite a hadith from Ibn 'Abbas allowing it. The Hanafi response is that the hadith refers to immediate transfer, but if ambiguous (as in typical ruqba), it is not a valid gift.
The passage reconciles by saying if immediate transfer is intended, it is valid by consensus; if suspended, it is invalid.
5
Understand the consensus point
If the grantor says 'my house is yours' (immediate) and then adds a condition of return after death, the gift is valid and the condition is void. This is the reconciliation between the two positions.
Thus the dispute is verbal: interpretation of the wording determines validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح العمرى للمعمر له حال حياته، ولورثته بعده، وهي أن يجعل داره له عمره وهو الموهوب له فإذا مات ترد عليه لا الرقبى أي إن مت فهو لك .... المعمر له الدار المعمر أي لا يصح هي قول الواهب إلخ العمري أي المعمار للعمر له مدة عمره وصح العمرى إلخ: بضم العين بوزن فعلى وهي اسم من العمر، فإذا صحت تكون المعمر له بفتح الميم الثانية، وهو الموهوب له حال حياته، ويكون لورثته بعده أي بعد موت المعمر له؛ لقوله عليه : "من أعمر عمري فهو للمعمر له في حياته ومماته ولا ترقبوا ومن أرقب شيئا فهو سبيل "الميراث" رواه أحمد وأبو داود والنسائي، وعند مالك والشافعي حملها في القديم هو للمعمر، ومنافعه للمعمر له لا لوارثه. (عناية، فتح) لا الرقبي إلخ: أي لا تصح الرقبي – بضم الراء -، وأشار إلى تفسيره بقوله: إن مت قبلك فهو لك وكذا إن مت قبلي فهي لي، وإنما لم يصرح المصنف بقوله: "وإن مت فهو لي احترازا عن حاجة ذكر موته، فكان كل واحد منهما يراقب موت الآخر، فلا يجوز؛ لما روينا وقال أبو يوسف يجوز؛ لما روي عن ابن عباس أنه عليه قال: "العمرى جائزة لمن أعمرها والرقبى جائزة لمن أرقبها"، رواه أحمد والنسائي، وبه قال الشافعي وأحمد، والجواب عنه: أنه مأخوذ من الإرقاب معناه: رقبة داري لك، وذلك جائز، لكن لما احتمل الأمرين، لم تثبت الهبة بالشك، فتكون عارية رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٧/٢] قال في "الفتح" عند قول الشارح ملا مسكين: لا يصح عندهما ؛ لعدم التمليك في الحال، وإذا لم تصح هبته، فهي عارية؛ لأنها تتضمن إطلاق الانتفاع، كذا في "الحموي" عن "الينابيع"، ثم قال في "الفتح" عند قول الشارح: "خلافا لأبي يوسف": وهو الأصح كما في غاية البيان" اعلم أن الخلاف بينهما وبين أبي يوسف لفظي، فقول أبي يوسف: "يجوز "الرقى بناء على أنها تمليك للحال واشتراط الاسترداد بعد موته فصح التمليك، وقولهما بعدم صحة الرقبي بناء على أن التمليك مضاف إلى زمان، فلا يصح؛ لعدم التمليك، كذا في "الدرر". فحاصله : أنه متى وجد التمليك في الحال، واشترط الرد في المآل يجوز بالإجماع؛ لما بينا أن الهبة لا تبطل بالشرط، بل الشرط يبطل، ومتى كان التمليك مضافا إلى زمان في المستقبل، لا يجوز ،بالإجماع، فكان الخلاف مبنيا على تفسير الرقبي، فمن قال: إنه تمليك في الحال أجازه ومن قال إنه مضاف لم يجزه، وبالجملة فقد ورد في العمرى والرقبى أخبار كثيرة بعضها بالمنع وبعضها بالجواز وبالحمل على ما حملناه يحصل التوفيق. (كذا في الزيلعي)
And the 'umra is valid for the recipient ('umra recipient) during his life, and for his heirs after him. It is that he makes his house for him for his lifetime. So when he dies, it returns. Not ruqba, meaning 'if you die before me, it is yours'. The recipient ('umra recipient) is the person gifted to. So it is not valid... 'umra with damm 'ayn, the form fu'la, it is derived from 'umr (life). When it is valid, the recipient (with fatha on the second mim) is the donee in his life, and it becomes for his heirs after him, i.e., after the death of the recipient, because of his statement: 'Whoever grants a life grant, it is for the recipient in his life and death, and do not do ruqba, and whoever does ruqba, it is upon the path of inheritance.' Narrated by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and Nasa'i. According to Malik and Shafi'i in the old view, it is for the grantor, and its benefits for the recipient, not for his heir. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:267). 'So when he dies etc.' It is not hidden what it contains of deficiency and suggestion of what is not intended. Clarified by the saying of al-Nuqaya and its commentary by al-Qahistani: 'Umra in shari'ah is making his house for him, i.e., the recipient, for the duration of his life, on condition that it returns to the grantor or his heirs. If the recipient dies or the grantor dies, he says: 'I grant you this house for your life, if you die it is mine, if I die it is for my heirs.' The shari'ah abrogated the condition, i.e., the condition of return to the grantor or his heirs as in Jahiliyyah. So the house is for the recipient in his life and for his heirs after his death. And the condition is void because the shari'ah abrogated it, and its invalidity does not affect the validity of the contract, as we have established that the gift is not invalidated by invalid conditions. ('Inayah, Fath). 'Not ruqba etc.': i.e., ruqba (with damm ra') is not valid. He indicated its interpretation by his saying: 'If you die before me, it is yours' and likewise 'if I die before you, it is mine.' He did not explicitly say 'and if I die it is mine' to avoid mentioning his death. So each of them awaits the other's death, so it is not allowed because of what we have narrated. Abu Yusuf says it is allowed because of the narration from Ibn 'Abbas that he said: 'Life grant is valid for whom it was given, and ruqba is valid for whom it was given.' (Narrated by Ahmad and Nasa'i). And al-Shafi'i and Ahmad hold this view. The answer is that it is derived from irqab meaning: 'my house is for you,' and that is allowed. But since it is ambiguous between two possibilities, the gift is not established by doubt, so it will be a loan (ariyah). (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:267). He said in al-Fath at the statement of the commentator Mulla Miskin: 'It is not valid according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) because of the lack of immediate ownership transfer. And if the gift is not valid, then it is a loan because it includes permission to use.' So in al-Hamawi from al-Yanabi'. Then he said in al-Fath at the commentator's statement: 'Contrary to Abu Yusuf,' and it is the more correct as in Ghayat al-Bayan. Know that the difference between them and Abu Yusuf is verbal. Abu Yusuf's view that ruqba is valid is based on it being an immediate ownership transfer with a condition of return after his death, so the transfer is valid. Their view that ruqba is not valid is based on the transfer being attached to a future time, so it is not valid due to lack of transfer. So in summary: whenever there is immediate transfer and a condition of return in the end, it is valid by consensus, because the gift is not invalidated by the condition, rather the condition is invalid. Whenever the transfer is attached to a future time, it is not valid by consensus. So the difference is based on the interpretation of ruqba. Whoever says it is an immediate transfer allows it; whoever says it is attached to a future time does not allow it. In short, many reports have come regarding 'umra and ruqba, some prohibiting and some allowing, and by interpreting as we have, harmony is achieved. (As in al-Zayla'i).
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (hadith) on 'umra and ruqba. 'Umra (life grant) is valid: the recipient owns the property during his life, and it passes to his heirs after his death. Any condition that it returns to the grantor is invalid. This is based on the hadith: 'Whoever grants a life grant, it is for the recipient in his life and death.' Ruqba (a grant where each party awaits the other's death) is not valid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two companions) because there is no immediate ownership transfer; it is ambiguous. Abu Yusuf considers it valid based on a hadith, but the majority hold that if it is immediate transfer with a condition of return, it is valid by consensus; if it is attached to a future event (like death), it is invalid. The commentator reconciles the narrations by interpreting ruqba as a valid immediate transfer if phrased as 'my house is yours' but if ambiguous, it becomes a loan (ariyah). Thus, the intra-madhhab dispute is verbal, depending on interpretation.

Key Terms
العمرى (al-'umra): A grant of property for the recipient's lifetime, with ownership passing to heirs after death.
الرقبى (al-ruqba): A grant where the grantor says: 'If you die before me, it is mine; if I die before you, it is yours.'
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending the transfer on a future event (like death).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens to a property given as 'umra after the recipient dies?
A) It returns to the grantor.
B) It passes to the recipient's heirs.
C) It becomes a charity.
D) It becomes the property of the state.

Key Takeaway
'Umra is valid and the property passes to heirs; ruqba is invalid due to ambiguity and is treated as a loan.

Mukatab's Death: Judgment Against Mother's 'Aqila Not 'Ajz
Ruling on qadi's judgment in a specific mukatab inheritance case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a qadi's judgment of blood money on the mother's 'aqila does not constitute a declaration of the mukatab's inability ('ajz), and state the Hanafi position on the dispute regarding recourse.

Core Idea
A qadi's ruling against the mother's 'aqila confirms the kitabah, not the mukatab's inability, preserving the possibility of freedom.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a nuanced scenario involving a mukatab who dies leaving a debt sufficient for his kitabah and a son who commits a crime. The qadi orders the blood money to be paid by the mother's 'aqila. At first glance, one might think this ruling indicates the mukatab's inability to pay the kitabah, but the Hanafi scholars argue otherwise. In this lesson, we will unpack the legal reasoning (ta'lil) behind this ruling and examine the internal Hanafi dispute about whether the mother's masters can later recoup the money from the father's masters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the key question
Read the passage and note the facts: a mukatab dies leaving a child from a free woman and a debt (not cash) sufficient for the kitabah. The child commits a crime, and the qadi orders the blood money to be paid by the mother's 'aqila. The key question: does this judgment imply the mukatab's inability?
The condition 'debt not cash' is critical.
2
Understand why the judgment does not imply 'ajz
The commentary explains that because the debt is not cash, the mukatab is not considered free until the kitabah is actually paid. The judgment against the mother's 'aqila is a confirmation (taqrir) of the kitabah, as it keeps the possibility of the mukatab's freedom open. The child's attachment to the mother's side is temporary; if the mukatab later pays, the wala' transfers to the father's side.
The ruling is based on istihsan, preserving the continuity of the kitabah.
3
Trace the reasoning: comparison with cash (capital)
The passage contrasts this scenario with one where the mukatab leaves cash: if cash were left, immediate payment would be possible, so the child would be attached to the father's masters directly, and the judgment would be different. The debt is considered property in terms of its outcome (mal bi i'tibar ma'alih), so it does not trigger immediate freedom.
This distinction is essential for understanding the ruling.
4
Recognize the dispute on recourse and its resolution
After the mukatab later becomes free, the mother's masters may seek to recoup the blood money from the father's masters. Later Hanafi scholars differed: al-Takmilah allowed recourse because they were compelled; al-Fath and al-Durr denied it. The detail from al-Nihayah and al-Mi'raj resolves the conflict by timing: no recourse if payment was during the mukatab's life (because freedom is retroactive only to the last part); recourse allowed if payment occurred after the father's death but before kitabah payment (because freedom is based on the father's lifetime).
This resolution is presented as the relied-upon view.
5
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Given a scenario with different timing, determine whether the mother's masters have recourse. For example, if the mukatab dies, his son commits a crime, and the qadi orders payment by mother's 'aqila before the father's death, then no recourse. If after father's death but before kitabah payment, then recourse is allowed.
Practice applying the detail-based rule to various facts.

(عيني) ولو ترك إلخ: يعني إذا مات مكاتب وترك ولدا من امرأة حرة، وترك دينا على الناس يفي ببدل الكتابة لا عينا، وجنى الولد فقضى القاضي بأرشه على موالي الأم لم يكن هذا القضاء قضاء بعجز المكاتب؛ لأن المكاتب وإن ترك مالا وهو الدين، لكنه لا يحكم بعتقه إلا عند أداء البدل، فكانت الجناية على موالي الأم، فإذا قضى به القاضي عليهم كان القضاء تقرير الكتابة؛ لأنها تقتضي إلحاق الولد بموالي الأم، وإيجاب العقل عليهم، لكن على وجه يحتمل أن يعتق المكاتب، فينجر الولاء إلى موالي الأب، فتبقى الكتابة على حالها، والقضاء بما يقرر حكم الكتابة لا يكون تعجيزا للمكاتب، ثم إذا أدى بعد ذلك بدل الكتابة عتق المكاتب، وظهر للابن ولاء في جانب الأب، فينجر إليه ولاؤه، والولاء كالنسب فلا يثبت للأم إلا عند تعذر إثباته من الأب، وهل يرجع موالي الأم بما عقلوا على موالي الأب أم لا؟ اختلفوا فيه، قال في "التكملة": وكانوا مضطرين فيما عقلوا، فلهم الرجوع بذلك على موالي الأب، ولا يرجعون على ولي الجناية تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٢٣/٨] وقال في "الفتح": إنه صرح في "الدر" بعدم الرجوع، وكذا في "الزيلعي"، وقال في "الدر المختار" أيضا: إنه رجوع ، قال في رد المحتار" بعد ذكر الاختلاف: نعم! ذكر في "النهاية" و"المعراج" تفصيلا يدفع المخالفة، وهو أنهم لا يرجعون على موالي الأب بما عقلوا من جناية الولد في حياة المكاتب؛ لأنه إنما حكم بعتقه في آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته، فلا يستند عتقه إلى أول عقد الكتابة، أما لو عقلوا عن جنايته بعد موت الأب قبل أداء البدل، رجعوا؛ لأن عتق الأب استند إلى حال حياته، فتبين أن ولاءه كان لموالي الأب من ذلك الوقت، وموالي الأم كانوا مجبورين على الأداء. ملتقط من مواد متعددة مواضع. من الكتب) حرة: أي معتقة كما يدل عليه قوله: "على عاقلة "الأم". ودينا إلخ أي ترك دينا وفاء، وإنما قيد بالدين؛ لأنه لو ترك عينا يفي ببدل الكتابة لا يتأتى القضاء بالإلحاق بالأم؛ إذ يمكن الوفاء في الحال، ويلحق الولد بموالي الأب، لا بخلاف الدين، فإنه مال باعتبار مآله فلا يحكم بعتقه حتى يؤدي البدل. كذا في ملا مسكين والفتح وغيره) وفاء: صفة "دينا" أي وترك دينا على الناس يفي ببدل الكتابة
And if he left a child from a free woman and a debt sufficient for his kitabah, and the child committed a crime, and he [the qadi] gave judgment for it against the 'aqila of the mother, that is not a judgment of the mukatab's inability. And if the masters of the mother and the father disputed about his wala', and he gave judgment for it to the masters of the mother, the qadi's judgment on wala' is one because they both become like one person; so if he judges the freedom of one of them at a time, he judges the freedom of the other at that time, so he becomes free and dies free. (Ayni) "And if he left" etc.: meaning if a mukatab dies and leaves a child from a free woman, and leaves a debt owed by people sufficient for the kitabah amount (not cash), and the child committed a crime, and the qadi gave judgment for its blood money against the masters of the mother, this judgment is not a judgment of the mukatab's inability; because the mukatab, even if he left property which is the debt, but he is not judged to be free except upon payment of the kitabah, so the crime is against the masters of the mother. When the qadi gave judgment against them, that judgment is a confirmation of the kitabah, because it requires attaching the child to the masters of the mother and imposing the blood money on them, but in a way that it is possible that the mukatab becomes free, then the wala' is transferred to the masters of the father, so the kitabah remains as it is, and the judgment that confirms the ruling of kitabah is not a declaration of inability for the mukatab. Then if he later pays the kitabah amount, the mukatab becomes free, and for the son wala' appears on the father's side, so his wala' is transferred to him. And wala' is like lineage, so it is not established for the mother except when establishing it from the father is impossible. And do the masters of the mother recoup what they paid as blood money from the masters of the father or not? They differed about it. It was said in al-Takmilah: They were compelled in what they paid, so they have recourse for that against the masters of the father, and they do not have recourse against the guardian of the crime. And it was said in al-Fath: He declared in al-Durr that there is no recourse, and likewise in al-Zayla'i. And it was said in al-Durr al-Mukhtar also that there is recourse. He said in Radd al-Muhtar after mentioning the difference: Yes! There is mentioned in al-Nihayah and al-Mi'raj a detail that removes the conflict, which is that they do not have recourse against the masters of the father for what they paid as blood money of the child during the life of the mukatab, because he is only judged to be free in the last part of his life, so his freedom is not based on the beginning of the kitabah contract. As for if they paid blood money for his crime after the death of the father before payment of the kitabah, they have recourse, because the father's freedom was based on his lifetime, so it becomes clear that his wala' belonged to the masters of the father from that time, and the masters of the mother were compelled to pay. (Summarized from various sources.) "Free": i.e., freed, as indicated by his statement "against the 'aqila of the mother." "And a debt" etc.: i.e., he left a debt sufficient for payment, and it is qualified by debt because if he left cash sufficient for the kitabah amount, the judgment of attachment to the mother would not be applicable, since payment could be made immediately, and the child would be attached to the masters of the father, unlike debt, because it is property in terms of its outcome, so he is not judged to be free until he pays the kitabah. (From Mulla Miskeen, al-Fath, etc.) "Sufficient": an attribute of "debt", i.e., he left a debt owed by people sufficient for the kitabah amount.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (talil) from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses a specific scenario: a mukatab dies, leaving a child from a free woman and a debt (not cash) sufficient to cover the kitabah. The child commits a crime, and the qadi imposes the blood money (arsh) on the 'aqila of the mother's masters. The key ruling is that this judgment does NOT constitute a declaration (qada') of the mukatab's inability ('ajz) to pay the kitabah. The reasoning ('illah) provided in the commentary is as follows: The mukatab left property (the debt), but he is not considered free until the kitabah is actually paid. Since the debt is not cash in hand, the child's crime is attached to the mother's side for blood money. The judgment by the qadi against the mother's masters actually confirms and upholds the kitabah (taqrir al-kitabah), because it implies that the child is linked to the mother's masters for the time being, but the possibility remains that the mukatab will later pay and become free, at which point the wala' would transfer to the father's masters. Therefore, the kitabah remains in effect. The passage further discusses the dispute among later Hanafi authorities (e.g., al-Takmilah, al-Fath, al-Durr al-Mukhtar) regarding whether the mother's masters can recoup the blood money they paid from the father's masters after the mukatab's freedom. The resolution from al-Nihayah and al-Mi'raj distinguishes: if the blood money was paid during the mukatab's lifetime, no recourse is allowed because the freedom is retroactive only from the last part of his life; but if paid after the father's death but before kitabah payment, recourse is allowed because the father's freedom is established from his lifetime. The passage also explains that the debt qualification is crucial: if the deceased had left cash, immediate payment would be possible, thus the child would be attached to the father's masters directly, and the judgment would be different. The author emphasizes that wala' (patronage) is like lineage (nasab) and cannot be established for the mother when the father's side is available.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
عاقلة (al-'aqilah): The group of paternal relatives who are liable to pay blood money (diya or arsh) for unintentional homicide or injury caused by a member of their group.
الولاء (al-wala'): The bond of patronage that arises between a master and a freed slave; the master inherits from the freed slave and has certain rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a mukatab dies leaving a debt sufficient for kitabah and a son who commits a crime, why does the qadi's judgment of blood money on the mother's 'aqila NOT constitute a judgment of the mukatab's inability?
A) Because the mukatab left no property at all.
B) Because the debt is not cash and the mukatab may later pay and become free, so the judgment actually confirms the kitabah.
C) Because the qadi's judgment was invalid.
D) Because the mother's masters waived their rights.

Key Takeaway
A qadi's judgment ordering blood money from the mother's 'aqila does not constitute a declaration of the mukatab's inability; it confirms the kitabah, and the question of recourse depends on the timing of the payment relative to the mukatab's death and freedom.

When a Qadi's Judgment on Wala' Is a Judgment of 'Ajz (Inability)
Ruling that qadi's decision on wala' in a direct dispute implies 'ajz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the scenario in which a qadi's decision on wala' constitutes a judgment of 'ajz, and distinguish it from cases where it does not, applying the talil reasoning.

Core Idea
A qadi's ruling on wala' in a direct dispute necessarily determines the mukatab's status: slave or free.

In this second scenario from the kitabah chapter, the qadi faces a direct dispute over wala' of the deceased child. The ruling he issues—giving wala' to the mother's masters—implicitly declares the mukatab unable (ta'jiz) and annuls the kitabah. Understanding this talil sharpens your ability to trace the necessary legal consequences of a judicial decision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
The father's masters and mother's masters dispute the wala' of a deceased child. The outcome depends on whether the mukatab died free or a slave.
This is a direct dispute over wala', not an ancillary issue.
2
Recognize the legal dependency
Wala' of the child is determined by the status of the mukatab (child's father) at death: if free, wala' goes to father's masters; if slave, to mother's masters.
Thus the wala' ruling presupposes a determination of the mukatab's status.
3
Infer the necessary implication
If the qadi gives wala' to the mother's masters, he must have judged that the mukatab died a slave. This implies the kitabah was annulled due to inability.
The ruling on wala' is therefore a judgment of 'ajz by necessary implication (darurah).
4
Contrast with indirect dispute
In the first scenario, the qadi ruled on blood money only, which did not directly determine wala' or the mukatab's status. That ruling did not constitute a judgment of 'ajz.
Direct vs. indirect dispute is the key distinction.
5
Note the internal ikhtilaf
If the mukatab dies without means and without a child, al-Iskaff says the kitabah is immediately annulled; Abu al-Layth says it remains until a qadi rules. This affects whether a third party can pay posthumously.
Both views are within the Hanafi school; the passage does not give tarjih.

(ملتقط) في ولائه أي في ولاء ولد المكاتب.  كتاب المكاتب 213 موت المكاتب وعجزه فهو قضاء بالعجز وما أدى المكاتب من الصدقات وعجز طاب السيده، وإن جنى أي بعجز المكاتب إلى مولاه حال كونه بالجناية عن الكتابة عبد فكاتبه سيّده جاهلا بها، فعجز على أحد بجناية المكاتب ما أدى إليه من الصدقات فهو قضاء إلخ: أي القضاء بالولاء لموالي الأم قضاء بعجز المكاتب، وفسخ الكتابة؛ لأن هذه الخصومة وقعت في نفس الولاء مقصودا، وثبوت الولاء لأحدهما يبتني على بقاء الكتابة وانتقاضها، فإنها إذا فسخت مات المكاتب عبدا، واستقر الولاء على موالي الأم، وإذا بقيت واتصل بها الأداء مات حرا، وانتقل الولاء إلى موالي الأب، فلما قضى القاضي بالولاء لموالي الأم، قضى بعجز المكاتب وموته عبدا؛ لأن من ضرورة كون الولاء لقوم الأم موت المكاتب عبدا؛ لأنه لو مات حرا، لانجر الولاء إليه من قوم الأم. عن وهذا الاختلاف بين مواليهما في الولاء راجع في الحقيقة إلى قيام الكتابة وانتقاضها، وهو مسألة مجتهد فيها، فينفذ القضاء فيه، ولو خرج الدين بعد ذلك القضاء يكون لمولى المكاتب ميراثا عن عبده صيانة للقضاء . نقضه هذا بخلاف المسألة الأولى، فإن القضاء فيها بالأرش على موالي الأم، ليس بقضاء لهم بالولاء الذي يبتني على بقاء الكتابة وانتقاضها، ولا القضاء بالأرش على موالي الأم؛ لبقاء الكتابة كما مفصلا. مر قال "العيني": وهذا كله فيما إذا مات المكاتب عن وفاء، فأديت منه، أو عن ولد فأداها، وأما إذا مات لا عن وفاء، ولا عن ولد، فاختلفوا في بقاء الكتابة، قال "الإسكاف": تنفسخ حتى لو تطوع إنسان بأداء بدل الكتابة عنه لا يقبل منه، وقال "أبو الليث": لا تنفسخ ما لم يقض بعجزه حتى لو تطوع به إنسان عنه قبل القضاء بالفسخ جاز، ويحكم بعتقه في آخر حياته هذا ما لخصته من الشروح والحواشي.
"The masters of the mother and the father etc.": The scenario of the issue: That the child mentioned in the first question died after the death of the mukatab, and the masters of the father and the masters of the mother disputed. The masters of the father said: The mukatab died free and the wala' is ours. The masters of the mother said: He died a slave and the wala' is ours. The qadi gave judgment that the wala' of the child belongs to the masters of the mother. So this judgment is a judgment of the mukatab's inability and an annulment of the kitabah. (Mulattaq) "in his wala'" i.e. in the wala' of the child of the mukatab. [PAGE_214] Book of Mukatab 213 Death of Mukatab and His Inability So it is a judgment of inability. And what the mukatab paid from sadaqat and then became unable, it is pure for his master. And if he committed a crime ... i.e. by the inability of the mukatab to his master while he is by the crime from the kitabah, a slave whom his master made a mukatab ignorant of it, then he became unable. ... So it is a judgment etc.: i.e. the judgment giving wala' to the masters of the mother is a judgment of the mukatab's inability and an annulment of the kitabah; because this dispute occurred concerning the wala' itself as an intended matter. The establishment of wala' for one of them is based on the continuation or annulment of the kitabah. For if it is annulled, the mukatab dies a slave and wala' is settled on the masters of the mother; and if it remains and payment is made, he dies free and wala' transfers to the masters of the father. So when the qadi gave judgment that wala' belongs to the masters of the mother, he judged the mukatab's inability and his death as a slave; because it is a necessary consequence of wala' belonging to the mother's people that the mukatab dies a slave; for if he died free, wala' would have been transferred from the mother's people to his own people. And this dispute between their masters over wala' essentially goes back to the existence or annulment of the kitabah, and it is a matter subject to ijtihad, so the judgment is enforceable. And if the debt appears after that judgment, it belongs to the master of the mukatab as inheritance from his slave, to protect the judgment. This is unlike the first issue, for the judgment in it of blood money against the masters of the mother is not a judgment for them of wala' which is based on the kitabah's continuation or annulment, nor is the judgment of blood money against the masters of the mother due to the continuation of the kitabah, as detailed. Al-'Ayni said: All this is if the mukatab dies with means (wafa') and it is paid from it, or with a child who pays it. But if he dies without means and without a child, they differed about the continuation of the kitabah. Al-Iskaff said: It is annulled such that even if someone volunteers to pay the kitabah amount for him, it is not accepted from him. Abu al-Layth said: It is not annulled as long as he is not judged unable, such that if someone volunteers to pay it for him before the judgment of annulment, it is permissible, and he is judged to be free at the end of his life. This is what I summarized from the commentaries and glosses.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage provides a second scenario that contrasts with the first. It is a ruling with reasoning (talil) explaining when a qadi's decision does constitute a judgment of 'ajz (inability) of the mukatab. The scenario: after the death of the mukatab, the child (born to a free woman) also dies. The masters of the father and the masters of the mother dispute over the wala' of the deceased child. The masters of the father claim the mukatab died free (so wala' belongs to them), while the masters of the mother claim he died a slave (so wala' belongs to them). If the qadi rules in favor of the mother's masters, that ruling is a judgment of the mukatab's inability and an annulment of the kitabah. The reasoning: this dispute goes directly to the wala' of the child, which depends on whether the mukatab died free or a slave. If the kitabah was fulfilled, the mukatab died free and wala' goes to the father's masters; if not, he died a slave and wala' stays with the mother's masters. Thus the qadi's decision necessarily determines the status of the mukatab. This is unlike the first scenario where the qadi only ruled on blood money, which did not directly determine wala'. The passage also discusses the ikhtilaf among Hanafi scholars regarding the death of a mukatab without assets and without a child to fulfill the kitabah. Al-Iskaff holds that the kitabah is immediately annulled, even if someone volunteers payment. Abu al-Layth holds that it remains until a qadi rules on 'ajz, and if someone pays before that, the mukatab is deemed free at the end of his life. This difference has practical implications.

Key Terms
تعجيز (ta'jīz): The legal declaration that a mukatab is unable to fulfill the kitabah contract, resulting in annulment and return to slavery.
الولاء (al-walā'): The right of patronage that passes from the master to the freed slave and then to his descendants, entailing inheritance rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the qadi's decision to give wala' of the deceased son to the mother's masters constitute a judgment of the mukatab's inability?
A) Because the mother's masters are more deserving.
B) Because wala' for the mother's masters requires that the mukatab died a slave, which means the kitabah was annulled.
C) Because the qadi explicitly said so.
D) Because the father's masters did not object.

Key Takeaway
A qadi's ruling on wala' in a direct dispute necessarily implies the status of the mukatab, thus it is a judgment of 'ajz.

Purity of Sadaqah Paid by Mukatab to Master
Ruling on the permissibility of sadaqat for the master after mukatab's inability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the purity of sadaqah paid by a mukatab to his master and explain the rationale of tabaddul al-milk (change of ownership) along with the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
A sadaqah paid by a mukatab becomes pure for the master after the mukatab's inability because ownership and effective control change.

This lesson explores a nuanced ruling in Hanafi fiqh: the permissibility (purity) of sadaqah that a mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission) has paid to his master when the mukatab subsequently becomes unable to fulfill the contract. The key legal principle is tabaddul al-milk (change of ownership), which the author applies to the change in the master's control over the slave's property. We will examine the evidence, the intra-Hanafi dispute, and a subtle objection raised by later scholars.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling and its Condition
Identify the act (sadaqah paid by mukatab to master) and the ruling: it becomes pure (tayyib) for the master. Note the condition: the master must be eligible to receive sadaqah at the time of payment.
The ruling is for after the mukatab's inability.
2
Locate the Dalil and the Principle of Tabaddul al-Milk
The author cites the hadith of Barīrah and the principle that change of ownership (tabaddul al-milk) changes the legal status of wealth. Explain how the hadith supports the analogy.
The hadith: 'It is sadaqah for her and a gift for us.'
3
Trace the Analogy: Hadith of Barīrah
The sadaqah given to Barīrah became a gift for the Prophet, demonstrating that the same item can change from sadaqah to permissible wealth when ownership changes. Similarly, the master's recovery of full control after the mukatab's inability constitutes a change in effective ownership.
4
Understand the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
If the mukatab becomes unable before payment, Muhammad holds the same ruling even if the master is wealthy, while Abu Yusuf disagrees. Note the basis of their difference: Muhammad relies on the change in control, Abu Yusuf on the master's eligibility at payment.
The majority view follows Muhammad.
5
Analyze the Objection and the Commentator's Preferred Response
The objection: the master already owned the slave's person, so there is no real change of ownership. The initial answer (subjugated ownership) is critiqued. The commentator prefers a response based on effective control (yad): before inability, the master lacked control over the property, after inability he gains it, so it is as if ownership changed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طاب لسيّده : اعلم أن ما أدى المكاتب إلى سيده من الصدقات والمولى ممن تحل له الصدقة، فهو طيب له؛ لأن الملك قد تبدل، وتبدل الملك كتبدل العين، فصار كعين أخرى، وإليه أشار النبي الله الله بقوله في حق بريرة ما : "هي لها صدقة ولنا هدية لكنه إن كان أداؤه قبل العجز ، فالمسألة إجماعية بين الأئمة، وإن عجز قبل الأداء إلى المولى، فكذلك الحكم عند محمد لله وإن كان غنيا، خلافا لأبي يوسف ومثل المولى في هذا الحكم الفقير إذا استغنى، فإنه يطيب له ما أخذ من الزكاة حال الفقر. وكذا ابن السبيل إذا أخذ الصدقة ثم وصل إلى ماله وفي يده الصدقة. وههنا إشكال وهو أن الرقبة كان للمولى فأنى يتحقق تبدل الملك؟ وأجيب بأن ملك الرقبة كان للمولى مغلوبا في مقابلة ملك السيد للمكاتب فإن المكاتب يمنع المولى عن التصرف في ملكه وليس للمولى أن يمنعه وبالعجز ينعكس ذلك، وليس ذلك إلا بتبدل الملك للمولى، وفي هذا الجواب نظر ؛ لأنا لا نسلم أن ذلك تبدل الملك، ولئن كان فلا نسلم أن مثل هذا التبدل بمنزلة تبدل العين، ولعل الأولى في الجواب أن يقال: المولى لم يكن له يد قبل العجز وحصل به فكأنه تبدل. (فتح وتكملة بزيادة)
"It is pure for his master": Know that what the mukatab pays to his master from sadaqat while the master is one to whom sadaqah is permissible, it is pure for him; because ownership has changed, and change of ownership is like change of substance, so it becomes like another substance. The Prophet ﷺ referred to this in his statement regarding Barīrah: "It is sadaqah for her and a gift for us." But if his payment was before inability, the issue is unanimous among the imams. And if he became unable before payment to the master, the ruling is the same according to Muhammad, even if [the master] is wealthy, contrary to Abu Yusuf. And similar to the master in this ruling is the poor person who becomes wealthy, for what he took from zakat as a poor person becomes pure for him. Likewise the wayfarer who takes sadaqah and then reaches his wealth while the sadaqah is in his hand. And here there is an objection: The slave's person (raqabah) belonged to the master, so how can change of ownership be realized? It was answered: The ownership of the slave's person was subjugated in consideration of the master's ownership of the mukatab's person, for the mukatab prevents the master from disposing of his ownership, and the master cannot prevent him. Upon inability, that is reversed. And that is nothing but a change of ownership for the master. But this answer is questionable; for we do not concede that this is a change of ownership, and even if it were, we do not concede that such a change is like a change of substance. Perhaps the better answer is to say: The master had no control before inability, and by it control was obtained, so it is as if ownership changed. (From al-Fath and al-Takmilah with additions.)
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling and reasoning on the purity (tayyib) of sadaqat (voluntary charity or zakat) paid by a mukatab to his master when the master is eligible to receive sadaqah. The key point: after the mukatab becomes unable (i.e., the kitabah is annulled), the money he had previously paid as charity to his master becomes pure (i.e., permissible and no longer considered sadaqah that the master must avoid). The reason given is 'change of ownership' (tabaddul al-milk). The commentator draws an analogy to the hadith of Barīrah, where the Prophet said the meat given as sadaqah to Barīrah became a gift (and thus permissible) for him. There is a discussion on whether the ruling applies when payment was before or after inability. Before inability: unanimous. After inability: Imam Muhammad holds the same even if the master is wealthy; Abu Yusuf differs. The commentator also addresses an objection: how can ownership change when the master already owned the slave's person? The first answer (in the text) is that the master's ownership was 'subjugated' (maghlub) during the kitabah because the mukatab had rights, and upon inability it becomes full ownership, akin to change. The commentator finds this answer weak and suggests a better answer: before inability, the master had no effective control (yad) over the property given as charity; after inability, he gets control, so it is as if the ownership changed. This reflects the Hanafi principle that actual possession and control affect the ruling of purity of wealth.

Key Terms
تبدل الملك (tabaddul al-milk): The legal concept that when ownership of an item changes hands, the item's legal status (e.g., as sadaqah) changes accordingly.
طيب (tayyib): Pure and permissible to use; in this context, the sadaqah becomes halal for the master after inability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the sadaqah paid by a mukatab become pure for his master after the mukatab's inability?
A) Because the master becomes poor.
B) Because the ownership changes due to the reversal of control over the slave.
C) Because the mukatab's payment was invalid.
D) Because the master returns the money.

Key Takeaway
The sadaqah paid by a mukatab becomes pure for the master after the mukatab's inability due to the change in effective ownership (tabaddul al-milk), analogous to the hadith of Barīrah; this ruling holds even if the master is wealthy according to Muhammad's position, which is the mufta bihi.

Partition with Absent or Minor Co-owners
Three scenarios where partition is not performed due to absence or minority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios where a judge must not partition jointly owned property and explain the Hanafi reasoning based on the need for representation.

Core Idea
Partition is a judgment; without both parties or their representatives, the judge cannot divide.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a practical problem: shared property with an absent heir or minor. The author rules that in three specific situations, the judge must not divide. The reasoning hinges on the nature of partition as qada (judgment) requiring both litigants or their representatives. Understanding these scenarios clarifies the Hanafi insistence on representation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
From the text, list the three cases where the judge does not divide: (1) co-owners are buyers and one is absent; (2) property is in the hand of an absent heir or a minor; (3) only one heir is present with an absent co-owner.
All three result in the same ruling: 'it is not divided'.
2
Distinguish between inheritance and purchase
In inheritance, the present heir can represent the deceased regarding property in his hand because the heir stands in the deceased's shoes. In purchase, each buyer owns individually, so the present cannot represent the absent.
This distinction is key to understanding the first scenario.
3
Understand the role of representation (khasm)
The judge needs a khasm (litigant) for each party. For absent co-owners, a representative must be appointed. Without one, the proof against the absent is invalid.
A guardian or agent fulfills this role for minors or absent heirs.
4
Analyze the case of a sole present heir
If only one heir is present, he is a representative for himself but not for the deceased or the absent. Thus there is no adversary to litigate against, so division is not possible.
Even if the sole heir proves everything, the judge cannot pass judgment without both sides.
5
Apply the principle to minors
A minor cannot be a litigant; someone must represent them (guardian). If no representative is appointed, the judge cannot divide.
The same applies if the property is held by a depositary or borrower.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Book of Partition. Issues of Partition. And the house is in their hands, and with them is an absent heir or a minor, the division is made at their request, and they appoint a representative, meaning the two heirs who proved, meaning the two heirs, meaning the partners in the property, the property, meaning but they appoint a representative or a guardian to receive his share. In most copies instead of 'their hands and with them' it is 'their hands and with them' in plural form. Al-Fath said: It is said that it is a mistake, and the correct is 'in their hands and with them' as in some copies. The answer is that by the plural the dual is intended on the order of His statement: 'Indeed, your hearts have inclined' (Quran 66:4), and it was objected that there is ambiguity unlike the verse. The permissibility of this intention when safe from confusion is argued. I say the evidence for intending the dual is what will come that it is not divided if it is in the hand of the absent heir, so the claim of ambiguity is denied. (Fath, abridged). Regarding purchase, there is a difference between inheritance and purchase. The heir's ownership is like his ownership such that he can return due to defect to the seller of the testator, and the seller of the testator can return due to defect what the testator sold, and he becomes deceived by the testator's purchase, such that if he had intercourse with a slave woman bought by his testator and she gave birth and then was claimed back, the heir can reclaim from the seller of his testator the price and the value of the child due to deception from his side. So one of them stands as a representative for the deceased regarding what is in his hand, and the other for himself, so the division becomes judgment in the presence of the litigants. As for ownership established by purchase, each of them due to his own action in his share, and therefore it cannot be returned due to defect to the seller of his seller, so the present one cannot stand as representative for the absent. Then the proof regarding the absent is established without a representative, so it is not accepted. As for when the property is in the hand of the absent heir, because division is a judgment against the absent by removing something from his hand without a representative for him, it is not permissible. Similarly if part is in his hand and the rest in the hand of the present, and similarly if it is in the hand of his depositary, borrower, or in the hand of a minor, because the depositary and minor are not representatives. There is no difference in this between establishing proof and not in the correct view. As for when one heir is present, because he has no adversary with him. Although he is a representative for himself, he is not a representative for the deceased or the absent. (Fath, Takmila).
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the chapter on partition (qismah). The author explains three scenarios where the judge must not divide shared property. First, if co-owners are present but with an absent heir or minor, division occurs only if they request it and appoint a representative for the absent. Second, if the co-owners are buyers (not heirs) and one is absent, or if the property is in the hands of an absent heir or minor, or if only one heir is present, the judge does not divide. The key reasoning is that partition is a form of judgment (qada), and judgment cannot be passed against an absent person without a representative. For inheritance, the present heir represents the deceased regarding the property in his hand, so division is valid. For purchase, each owner acts individually, so the present cannot represent the absent. Thus, the proof is based on the necessity of having both parties present or represented. The commentary resolves a variant reading in al-Kanz and clarifies that the three scenarios all yield the same answer: 'it is not divided.'

Key Terms
قسمة (qismah): Partition or division of jointly owned property into shares.
خصم (khasm): Adversary or litigant; in this context, a representative who can argue on behalf of an absent party.
وارث غائب (warith gha'ib): An heir who is not present at the time of division.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the judge not divide property when co-owners are buyers and one is absent, unlike when they are heirs?
A) Because buyers are not Muslims
B) Because the present buyer cannot represent the absent buyer in litigation
C) Because the absent buyer must be present in person
D) Because the property is not jointly owned

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires that all parties to a partition be present or represented; absence without representation prevents the judge from dividing.

Slaughter Purifies Non-Edible Animals’ Hide and Meat
Ruling on purification of meat and hide by slaughter, except human and pig

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purification by slaughter of non-edible animals, identify the exceptions, and explain the Shafi'i counter-argument and the intra-Hanafi dispute on tasmiya.

Core Idea
Slaughter purifies both hide and meat of non-edible animals (except human and pig), even if meat remains unlawful to eat.

When you slaughter an animal that is not normally eaten, does it become pure? According to the Hanafi school, yes—both the meat and hide become pure (tahir) and usable, though the meat still cannot be eaten. This ruling, built on analogy to tanning, was challenged by Imam al-Shafi'i and also debated within the Hanafi school over whether the tasmiya (saying Bismillah) is required. Let us examine the reasoning and practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling and exceptions
Locate the core statement: 'Slaughtering what is not eaten purifies its meat and hide.' Then note the two exceptions: the human (because of honor) and the pig (because of inherent impurity).
The ruling applies to all animals not eaten, including horses, donkeys, mules, predatory animals, etc.
2
Understand the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that slaughter’s primary effect is to make meat permissible; purification of hide is subsidiary and cannot happen without the primary effect. To the Hanafis, this would be like slaughter by a Magian, which is invalid.
The Shafi'i view relies on the principle that subsidiary rulings cannot stand when the primary purpose is absent.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response using analogy
Hanafis counter that slaughter removes impure fluids, directly purifying the hide, just as tanning does. The hide’s purification is independently intended, analogous to how consumption is intended for meat. Therefore, even if the meat is not halal, the hide becomes pure.
The analogy to tanning (dibagh) is key: both remove impurity, so they share the same effective cause (illa).
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute on tasmiya
Within the Hanafi school, some hold that mere slaughter suffices because it removes blood. Others require the tasmiya (Bismillah), because the purifier is valid Islamic slaughter (dhakah), which requires tasmiya. The latter is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view, as cited from al-Fath.
This dispute affects practice: if an animal is slaughtered without tasmiya, some Hanafis would consider its hide impure.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical outcome
The text concludes that slaughter with tasmiya purifies the hide of non-edible animals. The meat is also purified (tahir) but remains unlawful to eat. The hide can be used without further tanning, e.g., for a prayer mat or water skin.
Remember: pig and human are never purified by slaughter; pig hide requires tanning after slaughter (and remains impure?), human body is honored and not purified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذبح ما لا يؤكل يطهر لحمه وجلده إلا الآدمي والخنزير، وقال الشافعي: الذكاة لا تؤثر في جميع ذلك؛ لأن أثر الذكاة في إباحة اللحم أصل، وفي طهارته، وطهارة الجلد تبع، ولا تبع بدون الأصل، فصار نظير ذبح المجوس، وأن الذكاة مؤثرة في إزالة الرطوبات النجسة، فإذا زالت طهرت، كما في الدباغ، وهذا الحكم مقصود في الجلد كالتناول في اللحم، وفعل المجوسي غير معتد به فلابد من الدباغ، وكما يطهر لحمه، يطهر شحمه أيضا حتى لو وقع في الماء القليل لا يفسده. وهل يجوز الانتفاع به لغير الأكل؟ قيل: لا يجوز اعتبارا بالأكل، وقيل: يجوز كالزيت إذا خالطه شحم الميتة والزيت غالب، فإنه ينتفع به في غير الأكل، والخنزير لا يؤثر فيه الدباغ لنجاسته، والآدمي لكرامته، وفي رواية: لا يطهر بالذكاة لحم ما لا يؤكل لحمه والجلد يطهر هو الصحيح، وقد مر في كتاب الطهارة تكملة البحر الرائق: ٣٤١/٨] قال في "الدراية": واختلفوا في أن الموجب لطهارة ما لا يؤكل لحمه مجرد الذبح أو الذبح مع التسمية؟ قيل مجرد الذبح؛ لأنه مؤثر في إزالة الدم المسفوح، وقيل: الذبح مع التسمية؛ لأن المطهر هو الذكاة، ولا ذكاة بدون التسمية كذا في "الفتح".
And slaughtering what is not eaten purifies its meat and hide except the human and the pig. And al-Shafi'i said: Slaughtering does not affect any of that, because the effect of slaughtering is principally in making the meat permissible, and in its purification, and the purification of the hide is subsidiary, and there is no subsidiary without the original. So it becomes like the slaughtering of a Magian. And slaughtering is effective in removing the impure fluids, so when they are removed, it becomes pure, as in tanning. And this ruling is intended for the hide as consumption is for the meat. And the act of a Magian is not considered, so tanning is needed. And as its meat is purified, so is its fat, so even if it falls into a small amount of water, it does not corrupt it. Is it permissible to benefit from it for other than eating? It is said: not permissible, considering eating; and it is said: permissible, like oil mixed with fat of a carcass when oil is predominant, then it is used for other than eating. And the pig is not affected by tanning due to its impurity, and the human due to his honor. In one narration: The meat of what is not eaten is not purified by slaughtering, but the hide is purified; that is the correct view. And it has passed in the Book of Purification (completion from al-Bahr al-Raiq). He said in al-Diraya: They differed whether the cause of purification of the meat of what is not eaten is mere slaughter, or slaughter with the name (tasmiya). It is said: mere slaughter, because it is effective in removing the flowing blood. And it is said: slaughter with the name, because the purifier is the valid slaughter (dhakah), and there is no dhakah without the tasmiya, as in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of slaughtering (dhabh) on animals that are not lawful to eat. The Hanafi position is that slaughtering purifies both the meat and the hide of such animals, except for humans (due to their honor) and pigs (due to their inherent impurity). The Shafi'i view, presented for contrast, is that slaughtering has no effect on these because its primary purpose is to make meat permissible; the purification of the hide is subsidiary and cannot be achieved without the main purpose. The Hanafi response explains that slaughtering removes impure blood and fluids, and thus the hide becomes pure like in tanning. Additionally, the fat of such animals is also purified, so if it falls into a small amount of water, it does not contaminate it. There is a dispute about whether such animals can be used for non-eating purposes, with two opinions: one forbids it by analogy to eating, the other permits it like oil mixed with carcass fat where oil is predominant. The pig and human are excluded from this purification. A further intra-Hanafi dispute is noted: some say mere slaughter suffices, others require saying the tasmiya (Bismillah) with the slaughter, because the purifier is the valid Islamic slaughter (dhakah), which requires tasmiya. The correct view according to the text is that tasmiya is needed.

Key Terms
الذكاة (al-dhakah): Valid Islamic slaughter that includes proper method and tasmiya.
الدباغ (al-dibagh): Tanning, the process of treating an animal hide to make it pure and usable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which two animals are not purified by slaughtering according to the Hanafi school?
A) Dog and pig
B) Human and pig
C) Cat and mouse
D) Cow and sheep

Key Takeaway
Slaughtering (with tasmiya) purifies the hide and meat of non-edible animals, except humans and pigs, allowing their use without tanning.

Defects Invalidating Udhiyah: Major Defects and Ear/Tail/Eye Rulings
Ruling with proof on udhiyah defects, expanded with ear/tail/eye details.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four major defects that invalidate udhiyah and apply the Hanafi criteria for ear, tail, and eye defects to determine validity.

Core Idea
Sacrificial animals with major defects (obviously one-eyed, sick, lame, or emaciated) are invalid; ear/tail defects depend on how much remains.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq specifies which defects render an animal invalid for udhiyah (sacrifice). The foundational ruling comes from a hadith listing four clear defects, then the text expands to ear, tail, and eye defects, where the Hanafi school has detailed thresholds. Understanding these rulings is crucial for selecting a valid sacrificial animal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four prohibited defects
From the hadith of al-Bara' ibn 'Azib, the Prophet ﷺ prohibits four: al-'awra' (obviously one-eyed), al-mareedah (obviously sick), al-'arja' (obviously lame), and al-kaseerah (broken-boned with no marrow). State these in Arabic and English.
The hadith uses 'al-bayyin' to indicate the defect must be obvious.
2
Define each defect according to the Hanafi school
Use the commentary to understand the precise criteria: the one-eyed has lost one eye; the lame must walk on three legs and lift the fourth completely (if it places it down with a limp, it is acceptable); the emaciated cannot walk to the slaughter place.
Additional defects like squint and sunken eye are also mentioned in al-Khaniyyah.
3
Apply the ear/tail defect ruling
Determine validity based on whether most of the ear or tail remains. The key hadith from 'Ali lists muqabalah, mudabarah, sharqa', kharqa'. The Hanafi rule: if most remains, valid; if most is missing, invalid. Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on the threshold (Abu Hanifa: one-third; Sahibayn: more than half).
The mufta bihi is Sahibayn's view (more than half suffices), as per Abu al-Layth.
4
Analyze eye defects
'Cut most of the eye' is interpreted as loss of most vision. The text describes a method to measure vision loss by tying each eye and observing the distance from which the animal sees food. This determines if half or one-third of vision is lost.
Loss of more than half the vision invalidates the animal.
5
Distinguish between impermissibility and dislike
According to al-Fath, the ear deformities (muqabalah etc.) are disliked (karahah) rather than outright impermissible. The four major defects, however, are firmly prohibited (la yujzi').
This nuance affects the fatwa: an animal with an ear defect may still be valid but is best avoided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأضحية
٣٢٥
أحكام الأضحية
لا بالعمياء والعوراء والعجفاء والعرجاء ومقطوع أكثر الأذن أو الذنب أو السن هي الذاهبة العينين المهزولة التي لا تمشي إلى المنسك
أو العين أو الألية.
تكون
لا بالعمياء والعوراء إلخ أي لا يجوز أن يضحي بهذه الأربع؛ لما روي عن البراء بن عازب له أنه عليه قال: "أربع لا يجزين في الأضاحي العوراء البين ،عورها والمريضة البين ،مرضها والعرجاء البين ظلعها، والكسيرة التي لا تنقي"، رواه أبو داود والنسائي وجماعة أخر وصححه الترمذي، وفي "الحاوي" قال مشايخنا: العرجاء التي تمشي بثلاثة قوائم وتجافي الرابع عن الأرض، لا تجوز الأضحية بها، وإن كانت تضع الرابع على الأرض، وتستعين به إلا أنها تتمايل مع ذلك، وتضعه وضعا خفيفا يجوز، وإن كانت ترفعه رفعا أو تحمل المنكسر، لا تجوز، وفي "الخانية": وكذا الحولاء التي في عينها حول، ولا تجوز المنفسخة العين وهي التي غارت عينها. تكملة البحر الرائق: ۳۵۱/۸] والعوراء : هي الذاهبة إحدى العينين.
أن
ومقطوع أكثر الأذن الخ: لقول علي الله: "أمرنا النبي عليه أن نستشرف العين والأذن، وأن لا نضحي بمقابلة ولا مدابرة ولا شرقاء ولا خرقاء"، رواه أبو داود والترمذي وصححه، والمقابلة : قطع من مقدم أذنها، والمدابرة: قطع من مؤخرها، والشرقاء: أن يكون الخرق في أذنها ،طولا والخرقاء أن يكون عرضا، وقيد بقوله: "أكثر الأذن"؛ لأنه إذا بقي أكثرها جاز، وكذا أكثر الذنب؛ لأن للأكثر حكم الكل، وعن أبي حنيفة: أن الثلث إذا ذهب، وبقي الثلثان يجوز ، وإن ذهب أكثر من الثلث لا يجوز، ويروى عنه الربع؛ لأنه يحكي حكاية الكل، ويروى ذهاب الثلث مانع وقالا إذا بقي أكثر من النصف أجزأه وهو اختيار أبي الليث، وفي كون النصف مانعا روايتان عنه. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٩/٢] قال في "الفتح": وما في "العيني" من قوله : "لا يضحي بمقابلة ولا مدابرة ولا شرقاء ولا "خرقاء" معناه الكراهة لا عدم الجواز. أو العين إلخ عطف على "الأذن" أي لا يضحي بمقطوع أكثر العين والمراد بمقطوع أكثر العين مذهوب أكثر ضوء العين، فإن القطع غير ظاهر في العين؛ إذ لا يقال : مقطوع أكثر العين، بل يقال ذاهب أكثر العين، ويعرف ذهاب قدر النصف أو الثلث العين بأن تشد العين المعيبة بعد أن لا تعلف الشاة يوما أو يومين، ثم يقرب العلف من إليها قليلا قليلا فإذا رأته من موضع أعلم ذلك المكان ثم تشد العين الصحيحة، ويقرب العلف إلى الشاة قليلا قليلا حتى إذا رأته أعلم ذلك المكان، ثم يقدر ما بين الرؤية الأولى والثانية من المسافة، فإن كان المسافة بينهما الثلث فقد ذهب الثلث وبقي ،الثلثان وإن كان نصفا فقد ذهب النصف. (فتح، ملا مسكين)
Book of Sacrifice. Rulings of Sacrifice: Not with the blind, the one-eyed, the emaciated, the lame, nor the one cut off most of the ear or tail or tooth (the one that has lost both eyes, the emaciated that does not walk to the place of sacrifice), nor the eye or the rump. Then: 'Not with the blind...' etc., meaning it is not permissible to sacrifice with these four; due to what was narrated from al-Bara' ibn 'Azib that the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Four do not suffice in sacrifices: the obviously one-eyed, the obviously sick, the obviously lame, and the broken-boned that has no marrow.' Narrated by Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i, and others, and authenticated by al-Tirmidhi. In al-Hawi: Our scholars said: The lame that walks on three legs and lifts the fourth from the ground – sacrifice with it is not permissible. If it places the fourth on the ground and uses it but sways and places it lightly, it is permissible. If it raises it completely or carries the broken, it is not permissible. In al-Khaniyyah: Similarly, the squinting whose eye has a squint, and the sunken eye whose eye has sunk. Completion of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 351/8. And the one-eyed is the one who has lost one eye. And cut off most of the ear etc.: due to the saying of 'Ali: The Prophet ﷺ commanded us to examine the eye and ear, and not to sacrifice with a muqabalah, mudabarah, sharqa', or kharqa'. Narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi and authenticated. Muqabalah is cutting from the front of its ear, mudabarah from the back, sharqa' is a slit lengthwise, kharqa' is widthwise. He restricted it with 'most of the ear' because if most remains, it is permissible, and similarly most of the tail, because most has the ruling of the whole. From Abu Hanifa: If one-third has gone and two-thirds remain, it is permissible; if more than one-third goes, not permissible. It is also narrated from him: one-quarter, because it is considered like the whole; and it is narrated that one-third prevents. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If more than half remains, it suffices – this is the choice of Abu al-Layth. Regarding half preventing, there are two narrations from him. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 369/2] He said in al-Fath: What is in al-'Ayni from his statement: 'Do not sacrifice with muqabalah, mudabarah, sharqa', or kharqa' – its meaning is dislike (karahah), not impermissibility (adam al-jawaz). Or the eye etc. is conjunct to 'the ear', i.e., do not sacrifice with that whose most of the eye is cut. What is meant by 'cut off most of the eye' is the loss of most of the light of the eye, for cutting is not apparent in the eye; it is not said 'cut off most of the eye' but rather 'gone most of the eye'. The loss of half or one-third of the eye is known by tying the defective eye after not feeding the sheep for a day or two, then bringing food to it little by little; when it sees it from a place, mark that place, then tie the healthy eye and bring food to the sheep little by little until it sees it, then mark that place. Then measure the distance between the first and second sighting. If the distance between them is one-third, then one-third has gone and two-thirds remain; if half, then half has gone. (Fath, Mulla Miskin)
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on defects that invalidate an animal for udhiyah (sacrifice). The text begins by listing four defects: blind (in both eyes), one-eyed, emaciated (so thin it cannot walk to the slaughter place), and lame. It then expands to include animals with most of the ear, tail, or tooth cut off, and also the eye or the rump. The commentary explains that the first four defects are based on a hadith from al-Bara' ibn 'Azib where the Prophet ﷺ explicitly forbids four: obviously one-eyed, obviously sick, obviously lame, and broken-boned with no marrow. The commentary then provides detailed definitions: the one-eyed is one who has lost one eye; the emaciated is too weak to walk; the lame is defined by three conditions: if it walks on three legs and lifts the fourth entirely, it is invalid; if it places the fourth down but sways, it is valid. Additional defects from al-Khaniyyah: squinting eye and sunken eye are also invalid. For the ear, a hadith from 'Ali lists four types: muqabalah (cut from front), mudabarah (cut from back), sharqa' (slit lengthwise), and kharqa' (slit widthwise). The key is 'most of the ear' – if most of the ear remains, it is valid. For the tail, similarly most remaining is valid. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on thresholds: Abu Hanifa says if one-third is lost it is invalid, but two-thirds remaining is valid; another narration says one-quarter; and the Sahibayn say more than half remaining suffices. The commentary from al-Fath notes that according to al-'Ayni, these ear deformities are disliked (makruh) but not impermissible. For the eye, 'cut most of the eye' is interpreted as loss of most vision, not literal cutting. The method to determine the extent of vision loss involves tying each eye alternately and measuring the distance from which the animal sees food. This practical method is unique and shows Hanafi attention to precise measurement. The overall ruling: these defects render the sacrifice invalid or at least disliked, with detailed conditions.

Key Terms
العوراء (al-'awra'): The one-eyed animal; one who has lost one eye.
العرجاء البين ظلعها (al-'arja' al-bayyin zul'aha): The obviously lame animal that cannot walk normally; detailed definition includes walking on three legs and lifting the fourth.
مقطوع أكثر الأذن (maqtu' akthar al-udhun): An animal with more than half of its ear cut off; the ruling depends on the amount remaining.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The four major defects invalidate udhiyah; for ear/tail defects, the relied-upon view is that if more than half remains, the animal is valid.
